vertical-align:middle; padding:0 0 0 3px;"><span style="font-size:90%;padding-left:3px"></span>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n</td></tr>\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n</tbody></table>\n<p>The <b>River Thames</b>, (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="&#39;t&#39; in &#39;tie&#39;">t</span><span title="/\xc9\x9b/: &#39;e&#39; in &#39;dress&#39;">\xc9\x9b</span><span title="&#39;m&#39; in &#39;my&#39;">m</span><span title="&#39;z&#39; in &#39;zoom&#39;">z</span></span>/</a></span><small class="nowrap">&#160;(<span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap"><a href="/wiki/File:En-uk-Thames.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="About this sound" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" width="11" height="11" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="20" data-file-height="20" /></a>&#160;</span><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/cf/En-uk-Thames.ogg" class="internal" title="En-uk-Thames.ogg">listen</a></span></span>)</small></span> <a href="/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Help:Pronunciation respelling key"><i title="English pronunciation respelling"><span style="font-size:90%">TEMZ</span></i></a>) known alternatively in parts as <b>the Isis</b> is a river that flows through <a href="/wiki/Southern_England" title="Southern England">southern England</a> including <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a>. At 215 miles (346&#160;km), it is the longest river entirely in England and the <a href="/wiki/Longest_rivers_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Longest rivers of the United Kingdom">second longest in the United Kingdom</a>, after the <a href="/wiki/River_Severn" title="River Severn">River Severn</a>. \n</p><p>It flows through <a href="/wiki/Oxford" title="Oxford">Oxford</a> (where it is called <a href="/wiki/The_Isis" title="The Isis">the Isis</a>), <a href="/wiki/Reading,_Berkshire" title="Reading, Berkshire">Reading</a>, <a href="/wiki/Henley-on-Thames" title="Henley-on-Thames">Henley-on-Thames</a> and <a href="/wiki/Windsor,_Berkshire" title="Windsor, Berkshire">Windsor</a>. The lower reaches of the river are called the <a href="/wiki/Tideway" title="Tideway">Tideway</a>, derived from its long <a href="/wiki/Tidal_reach" class="mw-redirect" title="Tidal reach">tidal reach</a> up to <a href="/wiki/Teddington_Lock" title="Teddington Lock">Teddington Lock</a>. It rises at <a href="/wiki/Thames_Head" title="Thames Head">Thames Head</a> in Gloucestershire, and flows into the <a href="/wiki/North_Sea" title="North Sea">North Sea</a> via the <a href="/wiki/Thames_Estuary" title="Thames Estuary">Thames Estuary</a>. The Thames drains the whole of <a href="/wiki/Greater_London" title="Greater London">Greater London</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Its tidal section, reaching up to Teddington Lock, includes most of its London stretch and has a rise and fall of 7 metres (23&#160;ft). Running through some of the driest parts of mainland Britain and heavily abstracted for drinking water, the Thames\' discharge is low considering its length and breadth: the Severn has a discharge almost twice as large on average despite having a smaller drainage basin. In <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a>, the <a href="/wiki/River_Tay" title="River Tay">Tay</a> achieves more than double the Thames\' average discharge from a drainage basin that is 60% smaller.\n</p><p>Along its course are 45 <a href="/wiki/Navigation_lock" class="mw-redirect" title="Navigation lock">navigation locks</a> with accompanying weirs. Its <a href="/wiki/Drainage_basin" title="Drainage basin">catchment area</a> covers a large part of south-eastern and a small part of western England; the river is fed by 38 named tributaries.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> The river contains over 80&#160;islands. With its waters varying from freshwater to almost seawater, the Thames supports a variety of wildlife and has a number of adjoining <a href="/wiki/Sites_of_Special_Scientific_Interest" class="mw-redirect" title="Sites of Special Scientific Interest">Sites of Special Scientific Interest</a>, with the largest being in the remaining parts of the <a href="/wiki/North_Kent_Marshes" title="North Kent Marshes">North Kent Marshes</a> and covering 5,449 hectares (13,460 acres).<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div class="toctitle" lang="en" dir="ltr"><h2>Contents</h2><span href="#The_Thames_in_the_arts"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">The Thames in the arts</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-43"><a href="#Visual_arts"><span class="toclevel-1 tocsection-46"><a href="#Major_flood_events"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">Major flood events</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-47"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-51"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">12</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-52"><a href="#Further_reading"><span class="tocnumber">13</span> <span class="toctext">Further reading</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-53"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">14</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=River_Thames&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Father_Thames,_St_John%27s_Lock,_Lechlade.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Father_Thames%2C_St_John%27s_Lock%2C_Lechlade.jpg/220px-Father_Thames%2C_St_John%27s_Lock%2C_Lechlade.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Father_Thames%2C_St_John%27s_Lock%2C_Lechlade.jpg/330px-Father_Thames%2C_St_John%27s_Lock%2C_Lechlade.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Father_Thames%2C_St_John%27s_Lock%2C_Lechlade.jpg/440px-Father_Thames%2C_St_John%27s_Lock%2C_Lechlade.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1200" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Father_Thames,_St_John%27s_Lock,_Lechlade.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A statue of Old Father Thames by <a href="/wiki/Raffaelle_Monti" title="Raffaelle Monti">Raffaelle Monti</a> at <a href="/wiki/St_John%27s_Lock" title="St John&#39;s Lock">St John\'s Lock</a>, Lechlade</div></div></div>\n<p>The Thames, from Middle English <i>Temese</i>, is derived from the <a href="/wiki/Brittonic_languages" title="Brittonic languages">Brittonic Celtic</a> name for the river, <i>Tamesas</i> (from *<i>tam\xc4\x93ssa</i>),<sup id="cite_ref-MalloryAdams147_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MalloryAdams147-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> recorded in Latin as <i>Tamesis</i> and yielding modern Welsh <i>Tafwys</i> "Thames". The name may have meant "dark" and can be compared to other cognates such as Russian \xd1\x82\xd0\xb5\xd0\xbc\xd0\xbd\xd0\xbe (<a href="/wiki/Proto-Slavic" title="Proto-Slavic">Proto-Slavic</a> *<i>t\xc4\xadm\xc4\xadn\xc5\xad</i>), <a href="/wiki/Lithuanian_language" title="Lithuanian language">Lithuanian</a> <i>tamsi</i> "dark", <a href="/wiki/Latvian_language" title="Latvian language">Latvian</a> <i>tumsa</i> "darkness", <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Tamas_(philosophy)" title="Tamas (philosophy)">tamas</a></i> and Welsh <i>tywyll</i> "darkness" (<a href="/wiki/Proto-Celtic_language" title="Proto-Celtic language">Proto-Celtic</a> *<i>temeslos</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Middle_Irish" title="Middle Irish">Middle Irish</a> <i>teimen</i> "dark grey".<sup id="cite_ref-MalloryAdams147_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MalloryAdams147-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> The same origin is shared by countless other river names, spread across Britain, such as the <a href="/wiki/River_Tamar" title="River Tamar">River Tamar</a> at the border of <a href="/wiki/Devon" title="Devon">Devon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cornwall" title="Cornwall">Cornwall</a>, several <a href="/wiki/River_Tame_(disambiguation)" class="mw-redirect mw-disambig" title="River Tame (disambiguation)">rivers named Tame</a> in <a href="/wiki/The_Midlands" title="The Midlands">the Midlands</a> and <a href="/wiki/North_Yorkshire" title="North Yorkshire">North Yorkshire</a>, the <a href="/wiki/River_Tavy" title="River Tavy">Tavy</a> on <a href="/wiki/Dartmoor" title="Dartmoor">Dartmoor</a>, the <a href="/wiki/River_Team" title="River Team">Team</a> of the North East, the <a href="/wiki/River_Teifi" title="River Teifi">Teifi</a> and <a href="/wiki/River_Teme" title="River Teme">Teme</a> of <a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">Wales</a>, the <a href="/wiki/River_Teviot" title="River Teviot">Teviot</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Borders" title="Scottish Borders">Scottish Borders</a>, as well as one of the Thames\' tributaries called the <a href="/wiki/River_Thame" title="River Thame">Thame</a>.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Kenneth_H._Jackson" title="Kenneth H. Jackson">Kenneth H. Jackson</a> has proposed that the name of the Thames is not <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_languages" title="Indo-European languages">Indo-European</a> (and of unknown meaning),<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> while Peter Kitson suggested that it is Indo-European but originated before the <a href="/wiki/Celts" title="Celts">Celts</a> and has a name indicating "muddiness" from a root <i>*t\xc4\x81-</i>, \'melt\'.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Indirect evidence for the antiquity of the name \'Thames\' is provided by a Roman potsherd found at Oxford, bearing the inscription <i>Tamesubugus fecit</i> (Tamesubugus made [this]). It is believed that Tamesubugus\' name was derived from that of the river.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Tamese was referred to as a place, not a river in the <a href="/wiki/Ravenna_Cosmography" title="Ravenna Cosmography">Ravenna Cosmography</a> (c. AD 700).\n</p><p>The river\'s name has always been pronounced with a simple <i>t</i> /t/; the <a href="/wiki/Middle_English" title="Middle English">Middle English</a> spelling was typically <i>Temese</i> and the Brittonic form <i><a href="/wiki/Tamesis" class="mw-redirect" title="Tamesis">Tamesis</a></i>. A similar spelling from 1210, "Tamisiam", is found in the <a href="/wiki/Magna_Carta" title="Magna Carta">Magna Carta</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Thames_by_Anne_Seymour_Damer.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Thames_by_Anne_Seymour_Damer.JPG/220px-Thames_by_Anne_Seymour_Damer.JPG" width="220" height="79" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Thames_by_Anne_Seymour_Damer.JPG/330px-Thames_by_Anne_Seymour_Damer.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Thames_by_Anne_Seymour_Damer.JPG/440px-Thames_by_Anne_Seymour_Damer.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3660" data-file-height="1315" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Thames_by_Anne_Seymour_Damer.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sculpture of Tamesis. Downstream <a href="/wiki/Keystone_(architecture)" title="Keystone (architecture)">keystone</a> of the central arch of <a href="/wiki/Henley_Bridge" title="Henley Bridge">Henley Bridge</a></div></div></div>\n<p>The Thames through <a href="/wiki/Oxford" title="Oxford">Oxford</a> is sometimes called <a href="/wiki/The_Isis" title="The Isis">the Isis</a>. Historically, and especially in <a href="/wiki/Victorian_era" title="Victorian era">Victorian</a> times, gazetteers and cartographers insisted that the entire river was correctly named the Isis from its source down to <a href="/wiki/Dorchester_on_Thames" title="Dorchester on Thames">Dorchester on Thames</a> and that only from this point, where the river meets the Thame and becomes the "Thame-isis" (supposedly subsequently abbreviated to Thames) should it be so called. <a href="/wiki/Ordnance_Survey" title="Ordnance Survey">Ordnance Survey</a> maps still label the Thames as "River Thames or Isis" down to Dorchester. However, since the early 20th century this distinction has been lost in common usage outside of Oxford, and some historians suggest the name <i>Isis</i> is nothing more than a <a href="/wiki/Clipping_(morphology)" title="Clipping (morphology)">truncation</a> of <i>Tamesis</i>, the <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> name for the Thames. Sculptures titled <i>Tamesis</i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Isis" title="Isis">Isis</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Anne_Seymour_Damer" title="Anne Seymour Damer">Anne Seymour Damer</a> can be found on the <a href="/wiki/Henley_Bridge" title="Henley Bridge">bridge</a> at <a href="/wiki/Henley-on-Thames" title="Henley-on-Thames">Henley-on-Thames</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oxfordshire" title="Oxfordshire">Oxfordshire</a> (the original terracotta and plaster models were exhibited at the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Academy" class="mw-redirect" title="Royal Academy">Royal Academy</a>, <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a>, in 1785. They are now on show at the <a href="/wiki/River_and_Rowing_Museum" title="River and Rowing Museum">River and Rowing Museum</a> in Henley).<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Richard_Coates" title="Richard Coates">Richard Coates</a> suggests that while the river was as a whole called the Thames, part of it, where it was too wide to ford, was called *<i>(p)lowonida</i>. This gave the name to a settlement on its banks, which became known as <a href="/wiki/Londinium" title="Londinium">Londinium</a>, from the Indo-European roots *<i>pleu-</i> "flow" and *<i>-nedi</i> "river" meaning something like the flowing river or the wide flowing unfordable river.<sup id="cite_ref-coates_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-coates-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>For merchant seamen, the Thames has long been just the "London River". Londoners often refer to it simply as "the river" in expressions such as "south of the river".<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The river gives its name to three informal areas: the <a href="/wiki/Thames_Valley" title="Thames Valley">Thames Valley</a>, a region of England around the river between Oxford and West London; the <a href="/wiki/Thames_Gateway" title="Thames Gateway">Thames Gateway</a>; and the greatly overlapping <a href="/wiki/Thames_Estuary" title="Thames Estuary">Thames Estuary</a> around the tidal Thames to the east of London and including the waterway itself. <a href="/wiki/Thames_Valley_Police" title="Thames Valley Police">Thames Valley Police</a> is a formal body that takes its name from the river, covering three <a href="/wiki/Counties_of_England" title="Counties of England">counties</a>. In non-administrative use, the river\'s name is used in those of <a href="/wiki/Thames_Valley_University" class="mw-redirect" title="Thames Valley University">Thames Valley University</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thames_Water" title="Thames Water">Thames Water</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thames_Television" title="Thames Television">Thames Television</a>, publishing company <a href="/wiki/Thames_%26_Hudson" title="Thames &amp; Hudson">Thames &amp; Hudson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thameslink_(route)" class="mw-redirect" title="Thameslink (route)">Thameslink</a> (north-south railways passing through <a href="/wiki/Central_London" title="Central London">central London</a>) and <a href="/wiki/South_Thames_College" title="South Thames College">South Thames College</a>. An example of its use in the names of historic entities is the <a href="/wiki/Thames_Ironworks_and_Shipbuilding_Company" title="Thames Ironworks and Shipbuilding Company">Thames Ironworks and Shipbuilding Company</a>.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Administration">Administration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=River_Thames&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Administration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>The administrative powers of the <a href="/wiki/Thames_Conservancy" title="Thames Conservancy">Thames Conservancy</a> have been taken on with modifications by the <a href="/wiki/Environment_Agency" title="Environment Agency">Environment Agency</a> and, in respect of the Tideway part of the river, such powers are split between the agency and the <a href="/wiki/Port_of_London_Authority" title="Port of London Authority">Port of London Authority</a>.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Human_activity">Human activity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=River_Thames&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Human activity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>The marks of human activity, in some cases dating back to <a href="/wiki/British_Iron_Age" title="British Iron Age">Pre-Roman Britain</a>, are visible at various points along the river. These include a variety of structures connected with use of the river, such as navigations, bridges and <a href="/wiki/Watermill" title="Watermill">watermills</a>, as well as prehistoric <a href="/wiki/Burial_mound" class="mw-redirect" title="Burial mound">burial mounds</a>. A major maritime route is formed for much of its length for shipping and supplies: through the <a href="/wiki/Port_of_London" title="Port of London">Port of London</a> for international trade, internally along its length and by its connection to the British canal system. The river\'s position has put it at the centre of many events in British history, leading to it being described by <a href="/wiki/John_Burns" title="John Burns">John Burns</a> as "liquid history".\n</p><p>Two broad canals link the river to other <a href="/wiki/River_basin" class="mw-redirect" title="River basin">river basins</a>: the <a href="/wiki/Kennet_and_Avon_Canal" title="Kennet and Avon Canal">Kennet and Avon Canal</a> (<a href="/wiki/Reading,_Berkshire" title="Reading, Berkshire">Reading</a> to <a href="/wiki/Bath,_Somerset" title="Bath, Somerset">Bath</a>) and the <a href="/wiki/Grand_Union_Canal" title="Grand Union Canal">Grand Union Canal</a> (London to the Midlands). The Grand Union effectively bypassed the earlier, narrow and winding <a href="/wiki/Oxford_Canal" title="Oxford Canal">Oxford Canal</a> which also remains open as a popular scenic recreational route. Three further cross-basin canals are disused but are in various stages of reconstruction: the <a href="/wiki/Thames_and_Severn_Canal" title="Thames and Severn Canal">Thames and Severn Canal</a> (via <a href="/wiki/Stroud" title="Stroud">Stroud</a>), which operated until 1927 (to the west coast of England), the <a href="/wiki/Wey_and_Arun_Canal" title="Wey and Arun Canal">Wey and Arun Canal</a> to <a href="/wiki/Littlehampton" title="Littlehampton">Littlehampton</a>, which operated until 1871 (to the south coast), and the <a href="/wiki/Wilts_and_Berks_Canal" class="mw-redirect" title="Wilts and Berks Canal">Wilts and Berks Canal</a>.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Rowing_(sport)" title="Rowing (sport)">Rowing</a> and sailing clubs are common along the Thames, which is navigable to such vessels. <a href="/wiki/Kayaking" title="Kayaking">Kayaking</a> and <a href="/wiki/Canoeing" title="Canoeing">canoeing</a> also take place. Major annual events include the <a href="/wiki/Henley_Royal_Regatta" title="Henley Royal Regatta">Henley Royal Regatta</a> and <a href="/wiki/The_Boat_Race" title="The Boat Race">the Boat Race</a>, while the Thames has been used during two <a href="/wiki/Summer_Olympic_Games" title="Summer Olympic Games">Summer Olympic Games</a>: <a href="/wiki/1908_Summer_Olympics" title="1908 Summer Olympics">1908</a> (<a href="/wiki/Rowing_at_the_1908_Summer_Olympics" title="Rowing at the 1908 Summer Olympics">rowing</a>) and <a href="/wiki/1948_Summer_Olympics" title="1948 Summer Olympics">1948</a> (<a href="/wiki/Rowing_at_the_1908_Summer_Olympics" title="Rowing at the 1908 Summer Olympics">rowing</a> and <a href="/wiki/Canoeing_at_the_1948_Summer_Olympics" title="Canoeing at the 1948 Summer Olympics">canoeing</a>). Safe headwaters and reaches are a summer venue for organised swimming, which is prohibited on safety grounds in a stretch centred on <a href="/wiki/Central_London" title="Central London">Central London</a>.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_and_natural_aspects">Physical and natural aspects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=River_Thames&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Physical and natural aspects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:ThamesMarker.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/ThamesMarker.JPG/220px-ThamesMarker.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/ThamesMarker.JPG/330px-ThamesMarker.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/ThamesMarker.JPG/440px-ThamesMarker.JPG stone at the official source of the River Thames named <a href="/wiki/Thames_Head" title="Thames Head">Thames Head</a> near <a href="/wiki/Kemble,_Gloucestershire" title="Kemble, Gloucestershire">Kemble</a></div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Seven_Sources_emerge_point.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" Springs, Gloucestershire">Seven Springs</a> source</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Thames_Barrier_London.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Thames_Barrier_London.jpg/220px-Thames_Barrier_London.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Thames_Barrier_London.jpg/440px-Thames_Barrier_London.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1200" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Thames_Barrier_London.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Thames_Barrier" title="Thames Barrier">Thames Barrier</a> provides protection against floods</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Thames_Panorama,_London_-_June_2009.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Thames_Panorama%2C_London_-_June_2009.jpg/220px-Thames_Panorama%2C_London_-_June_2009.jpg" width="220" height="94" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Thames_Panorama%2C_London_-_June_2009.jpg/330px-Thames_Panorama%2C_London_-_June_2009.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Thames_Panorama%2C_London_-_June_2009.jpg/440px-Thames_Panorama%2C_London_-_June_2009.jpg 2x" data-file-width="10110" data-file-height="4324" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Thames_Panorama,_London_-_June_2009.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Thames passes by some of the sights of London, including the <a href="/wiki/Houses_of_Parliament" class="mw-redirect" title="Houses of Parliament">Houses of Parliament</a> and the <a href="/wiki/London_Eye" title="London Eye">London Eye</a></div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Cmglee_London_Richmond_aerial.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Cmglee_London_Richmond_aerial.jpg/170px-Cmglee_London_Richmond_aerial.jpg" width="170" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Cmglee_London_Richmond_aerial.jpg/255px-Cmglee_London_Richmond_aerial.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Cmglee_London_Richmond_aerial.jpg/340px-Cmglee_London_Richmond_aerial.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1536" data-file-height="2048" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Cmglee_London_Richmond_aerial.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Thames passing through the <a href="/wiki/London_Borough_of_Richmond_upon_Thames" title="London Borough of Richmond upon Thames">London Borough of Richmond upon Thames</a></div></div></div>\n<p>The usually quoted <a href="/wiki/River_source" title="River source">source</a> of the Thames is at <a href="/wiki/Thames_Head" title="Thames Head">Thames Head</a> (at <a href="/wiki/Ordnance_Survey_National_Grid" title="Ordnance Survey National Grid">grid reference</a> <span style="white-space: nowrap" class="plainlinks nourlexpansion"><a class="external text" href="https://tools.wmflabs.org/os/coor_g/?pagename=River_Thames&amp;params=ST980994_region%3AGB_scale%3A25000">ST980994</a></span>). This is about <span class="frac nowrap"><sup>3</sup>&#8260;<sub>4</sub></span> mile (1.2&#160;km)<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> north of <a href="/wiki/Kemble,_Gloucestershire" title="Kemble, Gloucestershire">Kemble</a> parish church in southern <a href="/wiki/Gloucestershire" title="Gloucestershire">Gloucestershire</a>, near the town of <a href="/wiki/Cirencester" title="Cirencester">Cirencester</a>, in the <a href="/wiki/Cotswolds" title="Cotswolds">Cotswolds</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>However, <a href="/wiki/Seven_Springs,_Gloucestershire" title="Seven Springs, Gloucestershire">Seven Springs</a> near <a href="/wiki/Cheltenham" title="Cheltenham">Cheltenham</a>, where the <a href="/wiki/River_Churn" title="River Churn">Churn</a> (which feeds into the Thames near <a href="/wiki/Cricklade" title="Cricklade">Cricklade</a>) rises, is also sometimes quoted as the Thames\' source,<sup id="cite_ref-BBC_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> as this location is furthest from the mouth, and adds some 14 miles (23&#160;km) to the length. At Seven Springs above the source is a stone with the Latin <a href="/wiki/Hexameter" title="Hexameter">hexameter</a> inscription "Hic tuus o Tamesine pater septemgeminus fons", which means "Here, O Father Thames, [is] your sevenfold source".<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Spring_(hydrology)" title="Spring (hydrology)">springs</a> at Seven Springs flow throughout the year, while those at Thames Head are only seasonal (a <a href="/wiki/Winterbourne_(stream)" title="Winterbourne (stream)">winterbourne</a>). The Thames is the longest river entirely in England, but the <a href="/wiki/River_Severn" title="River Severn">River Severn</a>, which is partly in Wales, is the <a href="/wiki/List_of_rivers_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="List of rivers of the United Kingdom">longest river</a> in the United Kingdom. As the River Churn, sourced at Seven Springs, is 14 miles (23&#160;km) longer than the Thames (from its traditional source at Thames Head to the confluence), the overall length of the Thames measured from Seven Springs, 229 miles (369&#160;km), is greater than the Severn\'s length 220 miles (350&#160;km). Thus, the "Churn/Thames" river may be regarded as the longest natural river in the United Kingdom.\n</p><p>The stream from Seven Springs is joined at <a href="/wiki/Coberley" title="Coberley">Coberley</a> by a longer tributary which could further increase the length of the Thames, with its source in the grounds of the <a href="/wiki/National_Star_College" title="National Star College">National Star College</a> at <a href="/wiki/Ullenwood" title="Ullenwood">Ullenwood</a>.\n</p><p>The Thames flows through or alongside <a href="/wiki/Ashton_Keynes" title="Ashton Keynes">Ashton Keynes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cricklade" title="Cricklade">Cricklade</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lechlade" title="Lechlade">Lechlade</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oxford" title="Oxford">Oxford</a>, <a href="/wiki/Abingdon-on-Thames" title="Abingdon-on-Thames">Abingdon-on-Thames</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wallingford,_Oxfordshire" title="Wallingford, Oxfordshire">Wallingford</a>, <a href="/wiki/Goring-on-Thames" title="Goring-on-Thames">Goring-on-Thames</a> and <a href="/wiki/Streatley,_Berkshire" title="Streatley, Berkshire">Streatley</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pangbourne" title="Pangbourne">Pangbourne</a> and <a href="/wiki/Whitchurch-on-Thames" title="Whitchurch-on-Thames">Whitchurch-on-Thames</a>, <a href="/wiki/Reading,_Berkshire" title="Reading, Berkshire">Reading</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wargrave" title="Wargrave">Wargrave</a>, <a href="/wiki/Henley-on-Thames" title="Henley-on-Thames">Henley-on-Thames</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marlow,_Buckinghamshire" title="Marlow, Buckinghamshire">Marlow</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maidenhead" title="Maidenhead">Maidenhead</a>, <a href="/wiki/Windsor,_Berkshire" title="Windsor, Berkshire">Windsor</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eton,_Berkshire" title="Eton, Berkshire">Eton</a>, <a href="/wiki/Staines-upon-Thames" title="Staines-upon-Thames">Staines-upon-Thames</a> and <a href="/wiki/Egham" title="Egham">Egham</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chertsey" title="Chertsey">Chertsey</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shepperton" title="Shepperton">Shepperton</a>, <a href="/wiki/Weybridge" title="Weybridge">Weybridge</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sunbury-on-Thames" title="Sunbury-on-Thames">Sunbury-on-Thames</a>, <a href="/wiki/Walton-on-Thames" title="Walton-on-Thames">Walton-on-Thames</a>, <a href="/wiki/Molesey" title="Molesey">Molesey</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thames_Ditton" title="Thames Ditton">Thames Ditton</a>. The river was subject to minor redefining and widening of the main channel around Oxford, Abingdon and Marlow before 1850, since when further cuts to ease navigation have reduced distances further.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Molesey" title="Molesey">Molesey</a> faces <a href="/wiki/Hampton,_London" title="Hampton, London">Hampton</a>, London, and in <a href="/wiki/Greater_London" title="Greater London">Greater London</a> the Thames passes <a href="/wiki/Hampton_Court_Palace" title="Hampton Court Palace">Hampton Court Palace</a>, <a href="/wiki/Surbiton" title="Surbiton">Surbiton</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kingston_upon_Thames" title="Kingston upon Thames">Kingston upon Thames</a>, <a href="/wiki/Teddington" title="Teddington">Teddington</a>, <a href="/wiki/Twickenham" title="Twickenham">Twickenham</a>, <a href="/wiki/Richmond,_London" title="Richmond, London">Richmond</a> (with a famous view of the Thames from Richmond Hill), <a href="/wiki/Syon_House" title="Syon House">Syon House</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kew" title="Kew">Kew</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brentford" title="Brentford">Brentford</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chiswick" title="Chiswick">Chiswick</a>, <a href="/wiki/Barnes,_London" title="Barnes, London">Barnes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hammersmith" title="Hammersmith">Hammersmith</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fulham" title="Fulham">Fulham</a>, <a href="/wiki/Putney" title="Putney">Putney</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wandsworth" title="Wandsworth">Wandsworth</a>, <a href="/wiki/Battersea" title="Battersea">Battersea</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chelsea,_London" title="Chelsea, London">Chelsea</a>. In <a href="/wiki/Central_London" title="Central London">central London</a>, the river passes <a href="/wiki/Pimlico" title="Pimlico">Pimlico</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vauxhall" title="Vauxhall">Vauxhall</a>, and then forms one of the principal axes of the city, from the <a href="/wiki/Palace_of_Westminster" title="Palace of Westminster">Palace of Westminster</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Tower_of_London" title="Tower of London">Tower of London</a>. At this point, it historically formed the southern boundary of the medieval city, with <a href="/wiki/Southwark" title="Southwark">Southwark</a>, on the opposite bank, then being part of <a href="/wiki/Surrey" title="Surrey">Surrey</a>.\n</p><p>Beyond central London, the river passes <a href="/wiki/Bermondsey" title="Bermondsey">Bermondsey</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wapping" title="Wapping">Wapping</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shadwell" title="Shadwell">Shadwell</a>, <a href="/wiki/Limehouse" title="Limehouse">Limehouse</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rotherhithe" title="Rotherhithe">Rotherhithe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Millwall" title="Millwall">Millwall</a>, <a href="/wiki/Deptford" title="Deptford">Deptford</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greenwich" title="Greenwich">Greenwich</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cubitt_Town" title="Cubitt Town">Cubitt Town</a>, <a href="/wiki/Blackwall,_London" title="Blackwall, London">Blackwall</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_Charlton" class="mw-redirect" title="New Charlton">New Charlton</a> and <a href="/wiki/Silvertown" title="Silvertown">Silvertown</a>, before flowing through the <a href="/wiki/Thames_Barrier" title="Thames Barrier">Thames Barrier</a>, which protects central London from flooding by <a href="/wiki/Storm_surge" title="Storm surge">storm surges</a>. Below the barrier, the river passes <a href="/wiki/Woolwich" title="Woolwich">Woolwich</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thamesmead" title="Thamesmead">Thamesmead</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dagenham" title="Dagenham">Dagenham</a>, <a href="/wiki/Erith" title="Erith">Erith</a>, <a href="/wiki/Purfleet" title="Purfleet">Purfleet</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dartford" title="Dartford">Dartford</a>, <a href="/wiki/West_Thurrock" title="West Thurrock">West Thurrock</a>, <a href="/wiki/Northfleet" title="Northfleet">Northfleet</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tilbury" title="Tilbury">Tilbury</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gravesend" title="Gravesend">Gravesend</a> before entering the <a href="/wiki/Thames_Estuary" title="Thames Estuary">Thames Estuary</a> near <a href="/wiki/Southend-on-Sea" title="Southend-on-Sea">Southend-on-Sea</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sea_level">Sea level</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=River_Thames&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Sea level">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Sediment cores up to 10 m deep collected by the British Geological Survey from the banks of the tidal River Thames contain geochemical information and fossils which provide a 10,000-year record of sea-level change.<sup id="cite_ref-The_application_of_\xce\xb413C,_TOC,_C/N_geochemistry_to_reconstruct_Holocene_relative_sea_levels_and_paleoenvironments_in_the_Thames_Estuary,_UK._16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_application_of_\xce\xb413C,_TOC,_C/N_geochemistry_to_reconstruct_Holocene_relative_sea_levels_and_paleoenvironments_in_the_Thames_Estuary,_UK.-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> Combined, this and other studies suggest that the Thames sea-level has risen more than 30 m during the Holocene at a rate of around 5\xe2\x80\x936&#160;mm per year from 10,000 to 6,000 years ago.<sup id="cite_ref-The_application_of_\xce\xb413C,_TOC,_C/N_geochemistry_to_reconstruct_Holocene_relative_sea_levels_and_paleoenvironments_in_the_Thames_Estuary,_UK._16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_application_of_\xce\xb413C,_TOC,_C/N_geochemistry_to_reconstruct_Holocene_relative_sea_levels_and_paleoenvironments_in_the_Thames_Estuary,_UK.-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> The rise of sea level dramatically reduced when the ice melt nearly concluded over the past 4,000 years. Since the beginning of the 20th century rates of sea level rise range from 1.22&#160;mm per year to 2.14&#160;mm per year.<sup id="cite_ref-The_application_of_\xce\xb413C,_TOC,_C/N_geochemistry_to_reconstruct_Holocene_relative_sea_levels_and_paleoenvironments_in_the_Thames_Estuary,_UK._16-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_application_of_\xce\xb413C,_TOC,_C/N_geochemistry_to_reconstruct_Holocene_relative_sea_levels_and_paleoenvironments_in_the_Thames_Estuary,_UK.-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Catchment_area_and_discharge">Catchment area and discharge</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=River_Thames&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Catchment area and discharge">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Tributaries_of_the_River_Thames" title="Tributaries of the River Thames">Tributaries of the River Thames</a></div>\n<p>The Thames River Basin District, including the Medway catchment, covers an area of 6,229 square miles (16,130&#160;km<sup>2</sup>).<sup id="cite_ref-The_Environment_Agency_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Environment_Agency-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> The river basin includes both rural and heavily urbanised areas in the east and northern parts while the western parts of the catchment are predominantly rural. The area is among the driest in the United Kingdom. Water resources consist of <a href="/wiki/Groundwater" title="Groundwater">groundwater</a> from aquifers and water taken from the Thames and its tributaries, much of it stored in large <a href="/wiki/Reservoir#Bank-side_reservoir" title="Reservoir">bank-side reservoirs</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-The_Environment_Agency_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Environment_Agency-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Thames itself provides two-thirds of London\'s drinking water while groundwater supplies about 40 per cent of public water supplies in the total catchment area. Groundwater is an important water source, especially in the drier months, so maintaining its quality and quantity is extremely important. Groundwater is vulnerable to surface pollution, especially in highly urbanised areas.<sup id="cite_ref-The_Environment_Agency_17-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Environment_Agency-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="The_non-tidal_section">The non-tidal section</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=River_Thames&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: The non-tidal section">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Locks_and_weirs_on_the_River_Thames" title="Locks and weirs on the River Thames">Locks and weirs on the River Thames</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Jubilee_weir.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Jubilee_weir.jpg/220px-Jubilee_weir.jpg" width="220" height="117" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Jubilee_weir.jpg/330px-Jubilee_weir.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Jubilee_weir.jpg/440px-Jubilee_weir.jpg 2x" data-file-width="447" data-file-height="237" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Jubilee_weir.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Jubilee_River" title="Jubilee River">Jubilee River</a> at <a href="/wiki/Slough_Weir" class="mw-redirect" title="Slough Weir">Slough Weir</a></div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:St_John%27s_Lock_and_Lechlade_in_background.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/St_John%27s_Lock_and_Lechlade_in_background.JPG/170px-St_John%27s_Lock_and_Lechlade_in_background.JPG" width="170" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/St_John%27s_Lock_and_Lechlade_in_background.JPG/255px-St_John%27s_Lock_and_Lechlade_in_background.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/St_John%27s_Lock_and_Lechlade_in_background.JPG/340px-St_John%27s_Lock_and_Lechlade_in_background.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1920" data-file-height="2560" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:St_John%27s_Lock_and_Lechlade_in_background.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>St John\'s Lock, near <a href="/wiki/Lechlade" title="Lechlade">Lechlade</a></div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:River_thames_oxford.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/River_thames_oxford.jpg/220px-River_thames_oxford.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/River_thames_oxford.jpg/330px-River_thames_oxford.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/River_thames_oxford.jpg/440px-River_thames_oxford.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1200" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:River_thames_oxford.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The River Thames in <a href="/wiki/Oxford" title="Oxford">Oxford</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Brooks, canals and rivers, within an area of 3,842 square miles (9,951&#160;km<sup>2</sup>),<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> combine to form 38 main tributaries feeding the Thames between its source and <a href="/wiki/Teddington_Lock" title="Teddington Lock">Teddington Lock</a>. This is the usual <a href="/wiki/Tidal_limit" class="mw-redirect" title="Tidal limit">tidal limit</a>; however, high spring tides can raise the head water level in the reach above Teddington and can occasionally reverse the river flow for a short time. In these circumstances, tidal effects can be observed upstream to the next lock beside <a href="/wiki/Molesey_Lock" title="Molesey Lock">Molesey weir</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> which is visible from the towpath and <a href="/wiki/Hampton_Court_Bridge" title="Hampton Court Bridge">bridge</a> beside <a href="/wiki/Hampton_Court_Palace" title="Hampton Court Palace">Hampton Court Palace</a>. Before Teddington Lock was built in 1810\xe2\x80\x9312, the river was tidal at peak spring tides as far as <a href="/wiki/Staines_upon_Thames" class="mw-redirect" title="Staines upon Thames">Staines upon Thames</a>.\n</p><p>In descending order, non-related tributaries of the non-tidal Thames, with river status, are the <a href="/wiki/River_Churn" title="River Churn">Churn</a>, <a href="/wiki/River_Leach" title="River Leach">Leach</a>, <a href="/wiki/River_Cole,_Wiltshire" title="River Cole, Wiltshire">Cole</a>, <a href="/wiki/River_Ray,_Wiltshire" title="River Ray, Wiltshire">Ray</a>, <a href="/wiki/River_Coln" title="River Coln">Coln</a>, <a href="/wiki/River_Windrush" title="River Windrush">Windrush</a>, <a href="/wiki/River_Evenlode" title="River Evenlode">Evenlode</a>, <a href="/wiki/River_Cherwell" title="River Cherwell">Cherwell</a>, <a href="/wiki/River_Ock" title="River Ock">Ock</a>, <a href="/wiki/River_Thame" title="River Thame">Thame</a>, <a href="/wiki/River_Pang" title="River Pang">Pang</a>, <a href="/wiki/River_Kennet" title="River Kennet">Kennet</a>, <a href="/wiki/River_Loddon" title="River Loddon">Loddon</a>, <a href="/wiki/River_Colne,_Hertfordshire" title="River Colne, Hertfordshire">Colne</a>, <a href="/wiki/River_Wey" title="River Wey">Wey</a> and <a href="/wiki/River_Mole,_Surrey" class="mw-redirect" title="River Mole, Surrey">Mole</a>. In addition, there are occasional backwaters and artificial cuts that form islands, <a href="/wiki/Distributary" title="Distributary">distributaries</a> (most numerous in the case of the <a href="/wiki/River_Colne,_Hertfordshire" title="River Colne, Hertfordshire">Colne</a>), and man-made distributaries such as the <a href="/wiki/Longford_River" title="Longford River">Longford River</a>. Three canals intersect this stretch: the <a href="/wiki/Oxford_Canal" title="Oxford Canal">Oxford Canal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kennet_and_Avon_Canal" title="Kennet and Avon Canal">Kennet and Avon Canal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wey_Navigation" class="mw-redirect" title="Wey Navigation">Wey Navigation</a>.\n</p><p>Its longest artificial secondary channel (cut), the <a href="/wiki/Jubilee_River" title="Jubilee River">Jubilee River</a>, was built between Maidenhead and Windsor for flood relief and completed in 2002.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The non-tidal section of the river is owned and managed by the <a href="/wiki/Environment_Agency" title="Environment Agency">Environment Agency</a>, which is responsible for managing the flow of water to help prevent and mitigate flooding, and providing for navigation: the volume and speed of water downstream is managed by adjusting the sluices at each of the weirs and, at peak high water, levels are generally dissipated over preferred flood plains adjacent to the river. Occasionally, flooding of inhabited areas is unavoidable and the agency issues flood warnings. Due to stiff penalties applicable on the non-tidal river, which is a drinking water source before treatment, <a href="/wiki/Sanitary_sewer_overflow" title="Sanitary sewer overflow">sanitary sewer overflow</a> from the many <a href="/wiki/Sewage_treatment_plant" class="mw-redirect" title="Sewage treatment plant">sewage treatment plants</a> covering the upper Thames basin is rare in the non-tidal Thames, which ensures clearer water compared to the river\'s tideway.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="The_tidal_section">The tidal section</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=River_Thames&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: The tidal section">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Tideway" title="Tideway">Tideway</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:London_Stone,_Staines_028.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/London_Stone%2C_Staines_028.jpg/170px-London_Stone%2C_Staines_028.jpg" width="170" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/London_Stone%2C_Staines_028.jpg/255px-London_Stone%2C_Staines_028.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/London_Stone%2C_Staines_028.jpg/340px-London_Stone%2C_Staines_028.jpg 2x" data-file-width="768" data-file-height="1024" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:London_Stone,_Staines_028.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/London_Stone_(riparian)" title="London Stone (riparian)">London Stone</a> at Staines, built in 1285 marked the customs limit of the Thames and the <a href="/wiki/City_of_London" title="City of London">City of London</a>\'s jurisdiction</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Thames_tide.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Thames_tide.jpg/220px-Thames_tide.jpg" width="220" height="111" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Thames_tide.jpg/330px-Thames_tide.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Thames_tide.jpg/440px-Thames_tide.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4947" data-file-height="2505" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Thames_tide.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Waterstand of Thames at low tide (left) and high tide (right) in comparison at Blackfriars Bridge in London</div></div></div>\n<p>Below Teddington Lock (about 55 miles or 89 kilometres upstream of the Thames Estuary), the river is subject to <a href="/wiki/Tide" title="Tide">tidal activity</a> from the <a href="/wiki/North_Sea" title="North Sea">North Sea</a>. Before the lock was installed, the river was tidal as far as Staines, about 16 miles (26&#160;km) upstream.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> London, capital of <a href="/wiki/Roman_Britain" title="Roman Britain">Roman Britain</a>, was established on two hills, now known as <a href="/wiki/Cornhill,_London" title="Cornhill, London">Cornhill</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ludgate_Hill" title="Ludgate Hill">Ludgate Hill</a>. These provided a firm base for a trading centre at the lowest possible point on the Thames.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>A river crossing was built at the site of <a href="/wiki/London_Bridge" title="London Bridge">London Bridge</a>. London Bridge is now used as the basis for published tide tables giving the times of <a href="/wiki/Tide" title="Tide">high tide</a>. High tide reaches Putney about 30 minutes later than London Bridge, and Teddington about an hour later. The <a href="/wiki/Tidal_reach" class="mw-redirect" title="Tidal reach">tidal stretch</a> of the river is known as "the <a href="/wiki/Tideway" title="Tideway">Tideway</a>". Tide tables are published by the <a href="/wiki/Port_of_London_Authority" title="Port of London Authority">Port of London Authority</a> and are available online. Times of high and low tides are also posted on Twitter.\n</p><p>The principal <a href="/wiki/Tributaries_of_the_River_Thames" title="Tributaries of the River Thames">tributaries of the River Thames</a> on the Tideway include the rivers <a href="/wiki/River_Brent" title="River Brent">Brent</a>, <a href="/wiki/River_Wandle" title="River Wandle">Wandle</a>, <a href="/wiki/River_Effra" title="River Effra">Effra</a>, <a href="/wiki/River_Westbourne" title="River Westbourne">Westbourne</a>, <a href="/wiki/River_Fleet" title="River Fleet">Fleet</a>, <a href="/wiki/River_Ravensbourne" title="River Ravensbourne">Ravensbourne</a> (the final part of which is called <a href="/wiki/Deptford_Creek" class="mw-redirect" title="Deptford Creek">Deptford Creek</a>), <a href="/wiki/River_Lea" title="River Lea">Lea</a>, <a href="/wiki/River_Roding" title="River Roding">Roding</a>, <a href="/wiki/River_Darent" title="River Darent">Darent</a> and <a href="/wiki/River_Ingrebourne" title="River Ingrebourne">Ingrebourne</a>. At London, the water is slightly <a href="/wiki/Brackish_water" title="Brackish water">brackish</a> with sea salt, being a mix of sea and fresh water.\n</p><p>This part of the river is managed by the <a href="/wiki/Port_of_London_Authority" title="Port of London Authority">Port of London Authority</a>. The flood threat here comes from high tides and strong winds from the North Sea, and the Thames Barrier was built in the 1980s to protect London from this risk.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/The_Nore" class="mw-redirect" title="The Nore">The Nore</a> is the <a href="/wiki/Sandbank" class="mw-redirect" title="Sandbank">sandbank</a>  that marks the mouth of the <a href="/wiki/Thames_Estuary" title="Thames Estuary">Thames Estuary</a>,  where the outflow from Thames meets the <a href="/wiki/North_Sea" title="North Sea">North Sea</a>. It is roughly halfway between <a href="/wiki/River_Roach#The_tidal_river" title="River Roach">Havengore Creek</a> in Essex and Warden Point on the <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Sheppey" title="Isle of Sheppey">Isle of Sheppey</a> in Kent. Until 1964 it marked the seaward limit of the Port of London Authority. As the sandbank was a major hazard for shipping coming in and out of London, in 1732 it received the world\'s first <a href="/wiki/Lightvessel" title="Lightvessel">lightship</a>. This became a major landmark, and was used as an assembly point for shipping. Today it is marked by Sea Reach No. 1 Buoy.<sup id="cite_ref-PortCities_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PortCities-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Islands">Islands</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=River_Thames&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Islands">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Islands_in_the_River_Thames" title="Islands in the River Thames">Islands in the River Thames</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Aerial_view_of_London_City_Airport_2007.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Aerial_view_of_London_City_Airport_2007.jpg/220px-Aerial_view_of_London_City_Airport_2007.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Aerial_view_of_London_City_Airport_2007.jpg/330px-Aerial_view_of_London_City_Airport_2007.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Aerial_view_of_London_City_Airport_2007.jpg/440px-Aerial_view_of_London_City_Airport_2007.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="683" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Aerial_view_of_London_City_Airport_2007.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/London_City_Airport" title="London City Airport">London City Airport</a> is on the site of a dock</div></div></div>\n<p>The River Thames contains over 80 islands ranging from the large estuarial marshlands of the <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Sheppey" title="Isle of Sheppey">Isle of Sheppey</a> and <a href="/wiki/Canvey_Island" title="Canvey Island">Canvey Island</a> to small tree-covered islets like <a href="/wiki/Rose_Isle" title="Rose Isle">Rose Isle</a> in Oxfordshire and <a href="/wiki/Headpile_Eyot" title="Headpile Eyot">Headpile Eyot</a> in Berkshire. They are found all the way from the <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Sheppey" title="Isle of Sheppey">Isle of Sheppey</a> in Kent to <a href="/wiki/Fiddler%27s_Island" title="Fiddler&#39;s Island">Fiddler\'s Island</a> in Oxfordshire. Some of the largest inland islands, for example <a href="/wiki/Formosa_Island" title="Formosa Island">Formosa Island</a> near Cookham and <a href="/wiki/Andersey_Island" title="Andersey Island">Andersey Island</a> at Abingdon, were created naturally when the course of the river divided into separate streams.\n</p><p>In the Oxford area the river splits into several streams across the <a href="/wiki/Floodplain" title="Floodplain">floodplain</a> (<a href="/wiki/Seacourt_Stream" class="mw-redirect" title="Seacourt Stream">Seacourt Stream</a>, <a href="/wiki/Castle_Mill_Stream" title="Castle Mill Stream">Castle Mill Stream</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bulstake_Stream" title="Bulstake Stream">Bulstake Stream</a> and others), creating several islands (<a href="/wiki/Fiddler%27s_Island" title="Fiddler&#39;s Island">Fiddler\'s Island</a>, <a href="/wiki/Osney" title="Osney">Osney</a> and others). <a href="/wiki/Desborough_Island" title="Desborough Island">Desborough Island</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ham_Island" title="Ham Island">Ham Island</a> at Old Windsor and <a href="/wiki/Penton_Hook_Island" title="Penton Hook Island">Penton Hook Island</a> were artificially created by lock cuts and navigation channels. <a href="/wiki/Chiswick_Eyot" title="Chiswick Eyot">Chiswick Eyot</a> is a landmark on the Boat Race course, while <a href="/wiki/Glover%27s_Island" title="Glover&#39;s Island">Glover\'s Island</a> forms the centre of a view from <a href="/wiki/Richmond_Hill,_London" title="Richmond Hill, London">Richmond Hill</a>.\n</p><p>Islands of historical interest include <a href="/wiki/Magna_Carta_Island" title="Magna Carta Island">Magna Carta Island</a> at <a href="/wiki/Runnymede" title="Runnymede">Runnymede</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fry%27s_Island" title="Fry&#39;s Island">Fry\'s Island</a> at Reading, and <a href="/wiki/Pharaoh%27s_Island,_River_Thames" title="Pharaoh&#39;s Island, River Thames">Pharaoh\'s Island</a> near Shepperton. In more recent times <a href="/wiki/Platts_Eyot" class="mw-redirect" title="Platts Eyot">Platts Eyot</a> at <a href="/wiki/Hampton,_London" title="Hampton, London">Hampton</a> was the place where <a href="/wiki/Motor_Torpedo_Boats" class="mw-redirect" title="Motor Torpedo Boats">Motor Torpedo Boats</a> (MTB)s were built, <a href="/wiki/Tagg%27s_Island" title="Tagg&#39;s Island">Tagg\'s Island</a> near Molesey was associated with the impresario <a href="/wiki/Fred_Karno" title="Fred Karno">Fred Karno</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eel_Pie_Island" title="Eel Pie Island">Eel Pie Island</a> at Twickenham was the birthplace of the South East\'s <a href="/wiki/R%26B" class="mw-redirect" title="R&amp;B">R&amp;B</a> music scene.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Westminster_Abbey" title="Westminster Abbey">Westminster Abbey</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Palace_of_Westminster" title="Palace of Westminster">Palace of Westminster</a> (commonly known today as the <a href="/wiki/Houses_of_Parliament" class="mw-redirect" title="Houses of Parliament">Houses of Parliament</a>) were built on <a href="/wiki/Thorney_Island_(London)" title="Thorney Island (London)">Thorney Island</a>, which used to be an <a href="/wiki/Eyot" class="mw-redirect" title="Eyot">eyot</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Geological_and_topographic_history">Geological and topographic history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=River_Thames&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Geological and topographic history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Ancestral_Thames" title="Ancestral Thames">Ancestral Thames</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Europe20000ya.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Europe20000ya.png/220px-Europe20000ya.png" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Europe20000ya.png/330px-Europe20000ya.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Europe20000ya.png/440px-Europe20000ya.png 2x" data-file-width="1900" data-file-height="1264" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Europe20000ya.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>European <a href="/wiki/Last_Glacial_Maximum" title="Last Glacial Maximum">LGM</a> refuges, 20,000 years ago. The Thames was a minor river that joined the <a href="/wiki/Rhine" title="Rhine">Rhine</a>, in the southern <a href="/w/index.php?title=North_Sea_basin&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="North Sea basin (page does not exist)">North Sea basin</a> at this time.<br /><div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #c54b00; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Solutrean" title="Solutrean">Solutrean</a> and Proto Solutrean Cultures</div><div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #ca00b0; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Epi <a href="/wiki/Gravettian" title="Gravettian">Gravettian</a> Culture</div></div></div></div>\n<p>The River Thames can first be identified as a discrete drainage line as early as 58&#160;million years ago, in the <a href="/wiki/Thanetian" title="Thanetian">Thanetian</a> stage of the late <a href="/wiki/Palaeocene" class="mw-redirect" title="Palaeocene">Palaeocene</a> epoch.<sup id="cite_ref-terriv_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-terriv-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> Until around 500,000 years ago, the Thames flowed on its existing course through what is now <a href="/wiki/Oxfordshire" title="Oxfordshire">Oxfordshire</a>, before turning to the north east through <a href="/wiki/Hertfordshire" title="Hertfordshire">Hertfordshire</a> and <a href="/wiki/East_Anglia" title="East Anglia">East Anglia</a> and reaching the <a href="/wiki/North_Sea" title="North Sea">North Sea</a> near <a href="/wiki/Ipswich" title="Ipswich">Ipswich</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>At this time the river system headwaters lay in the English <a href="/wiki/West_Midlands_(region)" title="West Midlands (region)">West Midlands</a> and may, at times, have received drainage from the <a href="/wiki/Berwyn_Mountains" class="mw-redirect" title="Berwyn Mountains">Berwyn Mountains</a> in <a href="/wiki/North_Wales" title="North Wales">North Wales</a>. Brooks and rivers like the <a href="/wiki/River_Brent" title="River Brent">River Brent</a>, <a href="/wiki/Colne_Brook" title="Colne Brook">Colne Brook</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bollo_Brook" class="mw-redirect" title="Bollo Brook">Bollo Brook</a> either flowed into the then River Thames or went out to sea on the course of the present-day River Thames.\n</p><p>About 450,000 years ago, in the most extreme <a href="/wiki/Ice_Age" class="mw-redirect" title="Ice Age">Ice Age</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Pleistocene" title="Pleistocene">Pleistocene</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Anglian_(stage)" class="mw-redirect" title="Anglian (stage)">Anglian</a>, the furthest southern extent of the ice sheet was at <a href="/wiki/Hornchurch" title="Hornchurch">Hornchurch</a> in east London.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> It dammed the river in <a href="/wiki/Hertfordshire" title="Hertfordshire">Hertfordshire</a>, resulting in the formation of large ice lakes, which eventually burst their banks and caused the river to be diverted onto its present course through what is now London. Progressively, the channel was pushed south to form the <a href="/wiki/St_Albans" title="St Albans">St Albans</a> depression by the repeated advances of the ice sheet.<sup id="cite_ref-nwriv_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nwriv-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>This created a new river course through <a href="/wiki/Berkshire" title="Berkshire">Berkshire</a> and on into London, after which the river rejoined its original course in southern <a href="/wiki/Essex" title="Essex">Essex</a>, near the present <a href="/wiki/River_Blackwater_(Essex)" class="mw-redirect" title="River Blackwater (Essex)">River Blackwater</a> estuary. Here it entered a substantial freshwater lake in the southern North Sea basin, south of what is called <a href="/wiki/Doggerland" title="Doggerland">Doggerland</a>. The overspill of this lake caused the formation of the <a href="/wiki/Channel_River" title="Channel River">Channel River</a> and later the <a href="/wiki/Strait_of_Dover" title="Strait of Dover">Dover Strait</a> gap between <a href="/wiki/Great_Britain" title="Great Britain">Britain</a> and France. Subsequent development led to the continuation of the course that the river follows at the present day.<sup id="cite_ref-nwriv_29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nwriv-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Most of the <a href="/wiki/Bedrock" title="Bedrock">bedrock</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Aylesbury_Vale" title="Aylesbury Vale">Vale of Aylesbury</a> is made up of <a href="/wiki/Clay" title="Clay">clay</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chalk" title="Chalk">chalk</a> that was formed at the end of the <a href="/wiki/Ice_age" title="Ice age">ice age</a> and at one time was under the <a href="/wiki/Ancestral_Thames" title="Ancestral Thames">Proto-Thames</a>. Also created at this time were the vast underground reserves of water that make the <a href="/wiki/Water_table" title="Water table">water table</a> higher than average in the Vale of Aylesbury.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Ice_age">Ice age</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=River_Thames&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Ice age">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Geological_map_of_London_Basin.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Geological_map_of_London_Basin.jpg/220px-Geological_map_of_London_Basin.jpg" width="220" height="200" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Geological_map_of_London_Basin.jpg/330px-Geological_map_of_London_Basin.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Geological_map_of_London_Basin.jpg/440px-Geological_map_of_London_Basin.jpg 2x" data-file-width="570" data-file-height="518" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Geological_map_of_London_Basin.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A geological map of the <a href="/wiki/London_Basin" title="London Basin">London Basin</a>; the London Clay is marked in dark brown</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Confluence_of_Rivers_Thames_and_Brent_-_geograph.org.uk_-_921332.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Confluence_of_Rivers_Thames_and_Brent_-_geograph.org.uk_-_921332.jpg/220px-Confluence_of_Rivers_Thames_and_Brent_-_geograph.org.uk_-_921332.jpg" width="220" height="168" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Confluence_of_Rivers_Thames_and_Brent_-_geograph.org.uk_-_921332.jpg/330px-Confluence_of_Rivers_Thames_and_Brent_-_geograph.org.uk_-_921332.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Confluence_of_Rivers_Thames_and_Brent_-_geograph.org.uk_-_921332.jpg/440px-Confluence_of_Rivers_Thames_and_Brent_-_geograph.org.uk_-_921332.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="489" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Confluence_of_Rivers_Thames_and_Brent_-_geograph.org.uk_-_921332.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The confluence of Rivers Thames and Brent. The narrowboat is heading up the <a href="/wiki/River_Brent" title="River Brent">River Brent</a>. From this point as far as <a href="/wiki/Hanwell" title="Hanwell">Hanwell</a> the Brent has been canalised and shares its course with the main line of the <a href="/wiki/Grand_Union_Canal" title="Grand Union Canal">Grand Union Canal</a>. From Hanwell the Brent can be traced to various sources in the <a href="/wiki/Chipping_Barnet" title="Chipping Barnet">Barnet</a> area.</div></div></div>\n<p>The last advance from that Scandinavian ice flow to have reached this far south covered much of north west <a href="/wiki/Middlesex" title="Middlesex">Middlesex</a> and finally forced the Proto-Thames to take roughly its present course. At the height of the last <a href="/wiki/Ice_age" title="Ice age">ice age</a>, around 20,000 BC, Britain was connected to mainland Europe by a large expanse of land known as <a href="/wiki/Doggerland" title="Doggerland">Doggerland</a> in the southern North Sea basin. At this time, the Thames\' course did not continue to Doggerland but flowed southwards from the eastern Essex coast where it met the <a href="/wiki/River_Rhine" class="mw-redirect" title="River Rhine">Rhine</a>, the <a href="/wiki/River_Meuse" class="mw-redirect" title="River Meuse">Meuse</a> and the <a href="/wiki/River_Scheldt" class="mw-redirect" title="River Scheldt">Scheldt</a><sup id="cite_ref-nwriv_29-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nwriv-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> flowing from what are now the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a> and <a href="/wiki/Belgium" title="Belgium">Belgium</a>. These rivers formed a single river \xe2\x80\x93 the <a href="/wiki/Channel_River" title="Channel River">Channel River</a> (<i>Fleuve Manche</i>) \xe2\x80\x93 that passed through the Dover Strait and drained into the Atlantic Ocean in the western <a href="/wiki/English_Channel" title="English Channel">English Channel</a>.\n</p><p>The ice sheet, which stopped around present day Finchley, deposited <a href="/wiki/Boulder_clay" title="Boulder clay">boulder clay</a> to form <a href="/wiki/Dollis_Hill" title="Dollis Hill">Dollis Hill</a> and Hanger Hill. Its torrent of <a href="/wiki/Meltwater" title="Meltwater">meltwater</a> gushed through the <a href="/wiki/Finchley_Gap" title="Finchley Gap">Finchley Gap</a> and south towards the new course of the Thames, and proceeded to carve out the <a href="/wiki/River_Brent" title="River Brent">Brent Valley</a> in the process.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> Upon the valley sides there can be seen other terraces of <a href="/wiki/Brickearth" title="Brickearth">brickearth</a>, laid over and sometimes interlayered with the clays.\n</p><p>These deposits were brought in by the winds during the periglacial periods, suggesting that wide, flat marshes were then part of the landscape, which the new river Brent proceeded to cut down. The steepness of the valley sides is an indicator of the very much lower <a href="/wiki/Mean_sea_level" class="mw-redirect" title="Mean sea level">mean sea levels</a> caused by the glaciation locking up so much water upon the land masses, thus causing the river water to flow rapidly seaward and so erode its bed quickly downwards.\n</p><p>The original land surface was around 350 to 400 feet (110 to 120 metres) above the current sea level. The surface had sandy deposits from an ancient sea, laid over sedimentary clay (this is the blue <a href="/wiki/London_Clay" title="London Clay">London Clay</a>). All the erosion down from this higher land surface, and the sorting action by these changes of water flow and direction, formed what is known as the Thames <a href="/wiki/Fluvial_terrace" title="Fluvial terrace">River Gravel Terraces</a>.\n</p><p>Since Roman times and perhaps earlier, the <a href="/wiki/Isostasy" title="Isostasy">isostatic</a> rebound from the weight of previous ice sheets, and its interplay with the <a href="/wiki/Eustatic" class="mw-redirect" title="Eustatic">eustatic</a> change in sea level, have resulted in the old valley of the River Brent, together with that of the Thames, silting up again. Thus, along much of the Brent\'s present-day course, one can make out the water meadows of rich alluvium, which is augmented by frequent floods.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Conversion_of_marshland">Conversion of marshland</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=River_Thames&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Conversion of marshland">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>After the river took its present-day course, many of the banks of the <a href="/wiki/Thames_Estuary" title="Thames Estuary">Thames Estuary</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Thames_Valley" title="Thames Valley">Thames Valley</a> in London were partly covered in <a href="/wiki/Marshland" class="mw-redirect" title="Marshland">marshland</a>, as was the adjoining <a href="/wiki/Lower_Lea_Valley" title="Lower Lea Valley">Lower Lea Valley</a>. Streams and rivers like the <a href="/wiki/River_Lea" title="River Lea">River Lea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tyburn_Brook" title="Tyburn Brook">Tyburn Brook</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bollo_Brook" class="mw-redirect" title="Bollo Brook">Bollo Brook</a> drained into the river, while some islands, e.g. <a href="/wiki/Thorney_Island_(London)" title="Thorney Island (London)">Thorney Island</a>, formed over the ages. The northern tip of the ancient parish of <a href="/wiki/Lambeth_(parish)" class="mw-redirect" title="Lambeth (parish)">Lambeth</a>, for example, was marshland known as <i>Lambeth Marshe</i>, but it was drained in the 18th century; it is remembered in the street name <a href="/wiki/Lower_Marsh" title="Lower Marsh">Lower Marsh</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ADMills_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ADMills-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/East_End_of_London" title="East End of London">East End of London</a>, also known simply as the <b>East End</b>, was the area of London east of the medieval walled <a href="/wiki/City_of_London" title="City of London">City of London</a> and north of the River Thames, although it is not defined by universally accepted formal boundaries; the <a href="/wiki/River_Lea" title="River Lea">River Lea</a> can be considered another boundary.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> Most of the local riverside was also marshland. The land was drained and became farmland; it was built on after the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a>. Use of the term "East End" in a pejorative sense began in the late 19th century,<sup id="cite_ref-ADMills_31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ADMills-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Canvey_Island" title="Canvey Island">Canvey Island</a> in southern Essex (area 18.45&#160;km<sup>2</sup>, 7.12&#160;sq&#160;mi; population 37,479<sup id="cite_ref-natstats_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-natstats-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>) was once marshy, but is now a fully reclaimed island in the Thames estuary. It is separated from the mainland of south <a href="/wiki/Essex" title="Essex">Essex</a> by a network of creeks. Lying below sea level it is prone to flooding at exceptional tides, but has nevertheless been inhabited since Roman times.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Wildlife">Wildlife</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=River_Thames&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Wildlife">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Swan_Upping.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Swan_Upping.jpg/220px-Swan_Upping.jpg" width="220" height="126" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Swan_Upping.jpg/330px-Swan_Upping.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Swan_Upping.jpg/440px-Swan_Upping.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1772" data-file-height="1012" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Swan_Upping.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Swan Upping \xe2\x80\x93 <a href="/wiki/Thames_skiff" title="Thames skiff">skiffs</a> surround the swans</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:PentonHIsle.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/PentonHIsle.JPG/220px-PentonHIsle.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/PentonHIsle.JPG/330px-PentonHIsle.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/PentonHIsle.JPG/440px-PentonHIsle.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="768" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:PentonHIsle.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Fishing at <a href="/wiki/Penton_Hook_Island" title="Penton Hook Island">Penton Hook Island</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Various species of birds feed off the river or nest on it, some being found both at sea and inland. These include <a href="/wiki/Great_cormorant" title="Great cormorant">cormorant</a>, <a href="/wiki/Black-headed_gull" title="Black-headed gull">black-headed gull</a> and <a href="/wiki/European_herring_gull" title="European herring gull">herring gull</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Mute_swan" title="Mute swan">mute swan</a> is a familiar sight on the river but the escaped <a href="/wiki/Black_swan" title="Black swan">black swan</a> is more rare. The annual ceremony of <a href="/wiki/Swan_Upping" title="Swan Upping">Swan Upping</a> is an old tradition of counting stocks.\n</p><p>Non-native geese that can be seen include <a href="/wiki/Canada_goose" title="Canada goose">Canada geese</a>, <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_goose" title="Egyptian goose">Egyptian geese</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bar-headed_goose" title="Bar-headed goose">bar-headed geese</a>, and ducks include the familiar native <a href="/wiki/Mallard" title="Mallard">mallard</a>, plus introduced <a href="/wiki/Mandarin_duck" title="Mandarin duck">Mandarin duck</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wood_duck" title="Wood duck">wood duck</a>. Other water birds to be found on the Thames include the <a href="/wiki/Great_crested_grebe" title="Great crested grebe">great crested grebe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_coot" title="Eurasian coot">coot</a>, <a href="/wiki/Moorhen" title="Moorhen">moorhen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Grey_heron" title="Grey heron">heron</a> and <a href="/wiki/European_kingfisher" class="mw-redirect" title="European kingfisher">kingfisher</a>.
