//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/River_Thames_Rubbish_Trap%2C_London_-_Dec_2008.jpg/440px-River_Thames_Rubbish_Trap%2C_London_-_Dec_2008.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4079" data-file-height="2382" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:River_Thames_Rubbish_Trap,_London_-_Dec_2008.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Rubbish" class="mw-redirect" title="Rubbish">Rubbish</a> traps are used on the Thames to filter <a href="/wiki/Debris" title="Debris">debris</a> as it flows through central London</div></div></div>\n<p>The Thames is maintained for navigation by powered craft from the estuary as far as <a href="/wiki/Lechlade" title="Lechlade">Lechlade</a> in Gloucestershire and for very small craft to <a href="/wiki/Cricklade" title="Cricklade">Cricklade</a>. The original <a href="/wiki/Towpath" title="Towpath">towpath</a> extends upstream as far as the connection with the now disused <a href="/wiki/Thames_and_Severn_Canal" title="Thames and Severn Canal">Thames and Severn Canal</a> at <a href="/wiki/Inglesham" title="Inglesham">Inglesham</a>, one and a half miles  upstream of the <a href="/wiki/St_John%27s_Lock" title="St John&#39;s Lock">last boat lock</a> near <a href="/wiki/Lechlade" title="Lechlade">Lechlade</a>. From Teddington Lock to the head of navigation, the navigation authority is the <a href="/wiki/Environment_Agency" title="Environment Agency">Environment Agency</a>. Between the sea and <a href="/wiki/Teddington_Lock" title="Teddington Lock">Teddington Lock</a>, the river forms part of the <a href="/wiki/Port_of_London" title="Port of London">Port of London</a> and navigation is administered by the <a href="/wiki/Port_of_London_Authority" title="Port of London Authority">Port of London Authority</a>. Both the tidal river through London and the non-tidal river upstream are intensively used for leisure navigation.\n</p><p>The non-tidal River Thames is divided into reaches by the 45 <a href="/wiki/Lock_(water_transport)" class="mw-redirect" title="Lock (water transport)">locks</a>. The locks are staffed for the greater part of the day, but can be operated by experienced users out of hours. This part of the Thames links to existing navigations at the <a href="/wiki/River_Wey_Navigation" class="mw-redirect" title="River Wey Navigation">River Wey Navigation</a>, the <a href="/wiki/River_Kennet" title="River Kennet">River Kennet</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Oxford_Canal" title="Oxford Canal">Oxford Canal</a>. All craft using it must be licensed. The <a href="/wiki/Environment_Agency" title="Environment Agency">Environment Agency</a> has patrol boats (named after tributaries of the Thames) and can enforce the limit strictly since river traffic usually has to pass through a lock at some stage. A speed limit of 8&#160;km/h (4.3&#160;kn) applies. There are pairs of <a href="/wiki/Navigation_transit_markers" title="Navigation transit markers">transit markers</a> at various points along the non-tidal river that can be used to check speed \xe2\x80\x93 a boat travelling legally taking a minute or more to pass between the two markers.\n</p><p>The tidal river is navigable to large ocean-going ships as far upstream as the <a href="/wiki/Pool_of_London" title="Pool of London">Pool of London</a> and <a href="/wiki/London_Bridge" title="London Bridge">London Bridge</a>. Although London\'s upstream enclosed docks have closed and central London sees only the occasional visiting <a href="/wiki/Cruise_ship" title="Cruise ship">cruise ship</a> or <a href="/wiki/Naval_ship" title="Naval ship">warship</a>, the tidal river remains one of Britain\'s main ports. Around 60 active terminals cater for shipping of all types including <a href="/wiki/Ro-ro" class="mw-redirect" title="Ro-ro">ro-ro</a> ferries, cruise liners and vessels carrying <a href="/wiki/Containerization" title="Containerization">containers</a>, vehicles, timber, grain, paper, <a href="/wiki/Petroleum" title="Petroleum">crude oil</a>, <a href="/wiki/Petroleum_products" class="mw-redirect" title="Petroleum products">petroleum products</a>, <a href="/wiki/Liquified_petroleum_gas" class="mw-redirect" title="Liquified petroleum gas">liquified petroleum gas</a> etc.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> There is a regular traffic of <a href="/wiki/Construction_aggregate" title="Construction aggregate">aggregate</a> or <a href="/wiki/Waste" title="Waste">refuse</a> vessels, operating from <a href="/wiki/Wharf" title="Wharf">wharves</a> in the west of London. The tidal Thames links to the canal network at the <a href="/wiki/River_Lea_Navigation" class="mw-redirect" title="River Lea Navigation">River Lea Navigation</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Regent%27s_Canal" title="Regent&#39;s Canal">Regent\'s Canal</a> at <a href="/wiki/Limehouse_Basin" title="Limehouse Basin">Limehouse Basin</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Grand_Union_Canal" title="Grand Union Canal">Grand Union Canal</a> at Brentford.\n</p><p>Upstream of <a href="/wiki/Wandsworth_Bridge" title="Wandsworth Bridge">Wandsworth Bridge</a> a speed limit of 8 knots (15&#160;km/h) is in force for powered craft to protect the riverbank environment and to provide safe conditions for rowers and other river users. There is no absolute speed limit on most of the Tideway downstream of Wandsworth Bridge, although boats are not allowed to create undue wash. Powered boats are limited to 12 knots between <a href="/wiki/Lambeth_Bridge" title="Lambeth Bridge">Lambeth Bridge</a> and downstream of Tower Bridge, with some exceptions. Boats can be approved by the <a href="/wiki/Harbourmaster" title="Harbourmaster">harbour master</a> to travel at speeds of up to 30 knots from below Tower Bridge to past the Thames Barrier.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="History_of_the_management_of_the_river">History of the management of the river</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=River_Thames&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: History of the management of the river">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>In the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a> <a href="/wiki/The_Crown" title="The Crown">the Crown</a> exercised general jurisdiction over the Thames, one of the four royal rivers, and appointed <a href="/wiki/Water_bailiff" title="Water bailiff">water bailiffs</a> to oversee the river upstream of Staines. The <a href="/wiki/City_of_London" title="City of London">City of London</a> exercised jurisdiction over the tidal Thames. However, navigation was increasingly impeded by <a href="/wiki/Weir" title="Weir">weirs</a> and mills, and in the 14th century the river probably ceased to be navigable for heavy traffic between Henley and Oxford. In the late 16th century the river seems to have been reopened for navigation from Henley to <a href="/wiki/Burcot,_Oxfordshire" title="Burcot, Oxfordshire">Burcot</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The first commission concerned with the management of the river was the <a href="/wiki/Oxford-Burcot_Commission" title="Oxford-Burcot Commission">Oxford-Burcot Commission</a>, formed in 1605 to make the river navigable between Burcot and Oxford.\n</p><p>In 1751 the <a href="/wiki/Thames_Navigation_Commission" title="Thames Navigation Commission">Thames Navigation Commission</a> was formed to manage the whole non-tidal river above Staines. The <a href="/wiki/City_of_London" title="City of London">City of London</a> long claimed responsibility for the tidal river. A long running dispute between the City and the Crown over ownership of the river was not settled until 1857, when the <a href="/wiki/Thames_Conservancy" title="Thames Conservancy">Thames Conservancy</a> was formed to manage the river from Staines downstream. In 1866 the functions of the Thames Navigation Commission were transferred to the Thames Conservancy, which thus had responsibility for the whole river.\n</p><p>In 1909 the powers of the Thames Conservancy over the <a href="/wiki/Tidal_river" title="Tidal river">tidal river</a>, below Teddington, were transferred to the <a href="/wiki/Port_of_London_Authority" title="Port of London Authority">Port of London Authority</a>.\n</p><p>In 1974 the Thames Conservancy became part of the new <a href="/wiki/Thames_Water_Authority" title="Thames Water Authority">Thames Water Authority</a>. When Thames Water was privatised in 1990, its river management functions were transferred to the <a href="/wiki/National_Rivers_Authority" title="National Rivers Authority">National Rivers Authority</a>, in 1996 subsumed into the <a href="/wiki/Environment_Agency" title="Environment Agency">Environment Agency</a>.\n</p><p>In 2010, the Thames won the world\'s largest environmental award at the time, the $350,000 International Riverprize, presented at the International Riversymposium in Perth, WA in recognition of the substantial and sustained restoration of the river by many hundreds of organisations and individuals since the 1950s.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_river_as_a_boundary">The river as a boundary</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=River_Thames&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: The river as a boundary">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Until enough crossings were established, the river presented a formidable barrier, with Belgic tribes and Anglo-Saxon kingdoms being defined by which side of the river they were on. When English counties were established their boundaries were partly determined by the Thames. On the northern bank were the ancient counties of <a href="/wiki/Gloucestershire" title="Gloucestershire">Gloucestershire</a>, Oxfordshire, <a href="/wiki/Buckinghamshire" title="Buckinghamshire">Buckinghamshire</a>, <a href="/wiki/Middlesex" title="Middlesex">Middlesex</a> and <a href="/wiki/Essex" title="Essex">Essex</a>. On the southern bank were the counties of <a href="/wiki/Wiltshire" title="Wiltshire">Wiltshire</a>, Berkshire, <a href="/wiki/Surrey" title="Surrey">Surrey</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kent" title="Kent">Kent</a>.\n</p><p>The 214 bridges and 17 tunnels that have been built to date have changed the dynamics and made cross-river development and shared responsibilities more practicable. In 1965, upon the creation of <a href="/wiki/Greater_London" title="Greater London">Greater London</a>, the <a href="/wiki/London_Borough_of_Richmond_upon_Thames" title="London Borough of Richmond upon Thames">London Borough of Richmond upon Thames</a> incorporated the former \'Middlesex and Surrey\' banks, <a href="/wiki/Spelthorne" title="Spelthorne">Spelthorne</a> moved from Middlesex to Surrey; and further changes in 1974 moved some of the boundaries away from the river. For example, some areas were transferred from Berkshire to Oxfordshire, and from Buckinghamshire to Berkshire. On occasion \xe2\x80\x93 for example in rowing \xe2\x80\x93 the banks are still referred to by their traditional county names.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Crossings">Crossings</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=River_Thames&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Crossings">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_crossings_of_the_River_Thames" title="List of crossings of the River Thames">List of crossings of the River Thames</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Newbridge,_Oxfordshire.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Newbridge%2C_Oxfordshire.jpg/220px-Newbridge%2C_Oxfordshire.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Newbridge%2C_Oxfordshire.jpg/330px-Newbridge%2C_Oxfordshire.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Newbridge%2C_Oxfordshire.jpg/440px-Newbridge%2C_Oxfordshire.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2048" data-file-height="1536" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Newbridge,_Oxfordshire.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Newbridge,_Oxfordshire" class="mw-redirect" title="Newbridge, Oxfordshire">Newbridge</a>, in rural Oxfordshire</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Railway_bridge_Maidenhead.jpeg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Railway_bridge_Maidenhead.jpeg/220px-Railway_bridge_Maidenhead.jpeg" width="220" height="89" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Railway_bridge_Maidenhead.jpeg/330px-Railway_bridge_Maidenhead.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Railway_bridge_Maidenhead.jpeg/440px-Railway_bridge_Maidenhead.jpeg 2x" data-file-width="531" data-file-height="214" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Railway_bridge_Maidenhead.jpeg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Railway bridge at <a href="/wiki/Maidenhead,_Berkshire" class="mw-redirect" title="Maidenhead, Berkshire">Maidenhead</a></div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:London_millennium_wobbly_bridge.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/London_millennium_wobbly_bridge.jpg/220px-London_millennium_wobbly_bridge.jpg" width="220" height="161" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/London_millennium_wobbly_bridge.jpg/330px-London_millennium_wobbly_bridge.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/London_millennium_wobbly_bridge.jpg/440px-London_millennium_wobbly_bridge.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1381" data-file-height="1011" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:London_millennium_wobbly_bridge.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Millennium_Bridge_(London)" class="mw-redirect" title="Millennium Bridge (London)">Millennium Footbridge</a> with <a href="/wiki/St_Paul%27s_Cathedral" title="St Paul&#39;s Cathedral">St Paul\'s Cathedral</a> in the background</div></div></div>\n<p>Many of the present-day road bridges are on the site of earlier fords, ferries and wooden bridges. At <a href="/wiki/Swinford_Bridge" class="mw-redirect" title="Swinford Bridge">Swinford Bridge</a>, a toll bridge, there was first a ford and then a ferry prior to the bridge being built. The earliest known major crossings of the Thames by the Romans were at <a href="/wiki/London_Bridge" title="London Bridge">London Bridge</a> and <a href="/wiki/Staines_Bridge" title="Staines Bridge">Staines Bridge</a>. At <a href="/wiki/Folly_Bridge" title="Folly Bridge">Folly Bridge</a> in Oxford the remains of an original Saxon structure can be seen, and medieval stone bridges such as <a href="/wiki/Newbridge,_Oxfordshire" class="mw-redirect" title="Newbridge, Oxfordshire">Newbridge</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wallingford_Bridge" title="Wallingford Bridge">Wallingford Bridge</a><sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Abingdon_Bridge" title="Abingdon Bridge">Abingdon Bridge</a> are still in use.\n</p><p>Kingston\'s growth is believed to stem from its having the only crossing between London Bridge and Staines until the beginning of the 18th century. During the 18th century, many stone and brick road bridges were built from new or to replace existing bridges both in London and along the length of the river. These included <a href="/wiki/Putney_Bridge" title="Putney Bridge">Putney Bridge</a>, <a href="/wiki/Westminster_Bridge" title="Westminster Bridge">Westminster Bridge</a>, <a href="/wiki/Datchet_Bridge" title="Datchet Bridge">Datchet Bridge</a>, <a href="/wiki/Windsor_Bridge" title="Windsor Bridge">Windsor Bridge</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sonning_Bridge" title="Sonning Bridge">Sonning Bridge</a>.\n</p><p>Several central London road bridges were built in the 19th century, most conspicuously <a href="/wiki/Tower_Bridge" title="Tower Bridge">Tower Bridge</a>, the only <a href="/wiki/Bascule_bridge" title="Bascule bridge">Bascule bridge</a> on the river, designed to allow ocean-going ships to pass beneath it. The most recent road bridges are the bypasses at <a href="/wiki/Isis_Bridge" title="Isis Bridge">Isis Bridge</a> and <a href="/wiki/Marlow_By-pass_Bridge" title="Marlow By-pass Bridge">Marlow By-pass Bridge</a> and the motorway bridges, most notably the two on the <a href="/wiki/M25_motorway" title="M25 motorway">M25</a> route <a href="/wiki/Queen_Elizabeth_II_Bridge" class="mw-redirect" title="Queen Elizabeth II Bridge">Queen Elizabeth II Bridge</a> and <a href="/wiki/M25_Runnymede_Bridge" title="M25 Runnymede Bridge">M25 Runnymede Bridge</a>.\n</p><p>Railway development in the 19th century resulted in a spate of bridge building including <a href="/wiki/Blackfriars_Railway_Bridge" title="Blackfriars Railway Bridge">Blackfriars Railway Bridge</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hungerford_Bridge" class="mw-redirect" title="Hungerford Bridge">Charing Cross (Hungerford) Railway Bridge</a> in central London, and the railway bridges by <a href="/wiki/Isambard_Kingdom_Brunel" title="Isambard Kingdom Brunel">Isambard Kingdom Brunel</a> at <a href="/wiki/Maidenhead_Railway_Bridge" title="Maidenhead Railway Bridge">Maidenhead Railway Bridge</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gatehampton_Railway_Bridge" title="Gatehampton Railway Bridge">Gatehampton Railway Bridge</a> and <a href="/wiki/Moulsford_Railway_Bridge" title="Moulsford Railway Bridge">Moulsford Railway Bridge</a>.\n</p><p>The world\'s first underwater tunnel was Marc Brunel\'s <a href="/wiki/Thames_Tunnel" title="Thames Tunnel">Thames Tunnel</a> built in 1843 and now used to carry the <a href="/wiki/East_London_Line" class="mw-redirect" title="East London Line">East London Line</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Tower_Subway" title="Tower Subway">Tower Subway</a> was the first railway under the Thames, which was followed by all the deep-level tube lines. Road tunnels were built in East London at the end of the 19th century, being the <a href="/wiki/Blackwall_Tunnel" title="Blackwall Tunnel">Blackwall Tunnel</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Rotherhithe_Tunnel" title="Rotherhithe Tunnel">Rotherhithe Tunnel</a>. The latest tunnels are the <a href="/wiki/Dartford_Crossing" title="Dartford Crossing">Dartford Crossings</a>.\n</p><p>Many foot crossings were established across the weirs that were built on the non-tidal river, and some of these remained when the locks were built \xe2\x80\x93 for example at <a href="/wiki/Benson_Lock" title="Benson Lock">Benson Lock</a>. Others were replaced by a footbridge when the weir was removed as at <a href="/wiki/Hart%27s_Weir_Footbridge" title="Hart&#39;s Weir Footbridge">Hart\'s Weir Footbridge</a>. Around 2000, several footbridges were added along the Thames, either as part of the Thames Path or in commemoration of the millennium. These include <a href="/wiki/Temple_Footbridge" title="Temple Footbridge">Temple Footbridge</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bloomers_Hole_Footbridge" title="Bloomers Hole Footbridge">Bloomers Hole Footbridge</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Hungerford_Footbridge" class="mw-redirect" title="Hungerford Footbridge">Hungerford Footbridges</a> and the <a href="/wiki/London_Millennium_Bridge" class="mw-redirect" title="London Millennium Bridge">Millennium Bridge</a>, all of which have distinctive design characteristics.\n</p><p>Before bridges were built, the main means of crossing the river was by ferry. A significant number of ferries were provided specifically for navigation purposes. When the <a href="/wiki/Towpath" title="Towpath">towpath</a> changed sides, it was necessary to take the towing horse and its driver across the river. This was no longer necessary when barges were powered by steam. Some ferries still operate on the river. The <a href="/wiki/Woolwich_Ferry" title="Woolwich Ferry">Woolwich Ferry</a> carries cars and passengers across the river in the Thames Gateway and links the <a href="/wiki/North_Circular" class="mw-redirect" title="North Circular">North Circular</a> and <a href="/wiki/South_Circular_Road,_London" title="South Circular Road, London">South Circular</a> roads. Upstream are smaller pedestrian ferries, for example <a href="/wiki/Hampton_Ferry_(River_Thames)" title="Hampton Ferry (River Thames)">Hampton Ferry</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shepperton_to_Weybridge_Ferry" title="Shepperton to Weybridge Ferry">Shepperton to Weybridge Ferry</a> the last being the only non-permanent crossing that remains on the Thames Path.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Pollution">Pollution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=River_Thames&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Pollution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Treated_sewage">Treated sewage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=River_Thames&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Treated sewage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Treated sewage from all the towns and villages in the Thames catchment flow into the Thames via sewage treatment plants. This includes all the sewage from Swindon, Oxford, Reading and Windsor.\n</p><p>However, untreated sewage still regularly enters the Thames during wet weather. When <a href="/wiki/London_sewerage_system" title="London sewerage system">London\'s sewerage system</a> was built, sewers were designed to overflow through discharge points along the river during heavy storms. Originally, this would happen once or twice a year, however overflows now happen once a week on average.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> In 2013, over 55m tonnes of raw sewage was washed into the tidal Thames. These discharge events kill fish, leave raw sewage on the riverbanks, and decrease the water quality of the river.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>To prevent the release of raw <a href="/wiki/Sewage" title="Sewage">sewage</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rainwater" class="mw-redirect" title="Rainwater">rainwater</a> into the river, the Thames Tideway Scheme is currently under construction at a cost of \xc2\xa34.2 billion. This project will collect sewage before it <a href="/wiki/Combined_sewage_overflow" class="mw-redirect" title="Combined sewage overflow">overflows</a>, before channeling it down a 25&#160;km (15&#160;mi) tunnel underneath the tidal Thames, so it can be treated at <a href="/wiki/Beckton_Sewage_Treatment_Works" class="mw-redirect" title="Beckton Sewage Treatment Works">Beckton Sewage Treatment Works</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> The result of the project will be reduction of sewage discharges into the river by 90%, dramatically increasing water quality.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mercury_levels">Mercury levels</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=River_Thames&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Mercury levels">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Mercury_(element)" title="Mercury (element)">Mercury</a> (Hg) is an environmentally persistent heavy metal which at high concentrations can be toxic to <a href="/wiki/Marine_life" title="Marine life">marine life</a> and humans. Sixty sediment cores of 1 m in depth, spanning the entire tidal River Thames, between <a href="/wiki/Brentford" title="Brentford">Brentford</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Grain" title="Isle of Grain">Isle of Grain</a> have been analysed for total Hg. The sediment records show a clear rise and fall of Hg pollution through history.<sup id="cite_ref-Rise_and_fall_of_Mercury_(Hg)_pollution_in_sediment_cores_of_the_Thames_Estuary,_London,_UK._69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rise_and_fall_of_Mercury_(Hg)_pollution_in_sediment_cores_of_the_Thames_Estuary,_London,_UK.-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> Mercury concentrations in the River Thames decrease downstream from London to the outer Estuary with the total Hg levels ranging from 0.01 to 12.07&#160;mg/kg, giving a mean of 2.10&#160;mg/kg which is higher than many other UK and European river estuaries.<sup id="cite_ref-Mercury_contamination_in_surface_sediments_and_sediment_cores_of_the_Mersey_Estuary,_UK._70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mercury_contamination_in_surface_sediments_and_sediment_cores_of_the_Mersey_Estuary,_UK.-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Rise_and_fall_of_Mercury_(Hg)_pollution_in_sediment_cores_of_the_Thames_Estuary,_London,_UK._69-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rise_and_fall_of_Mercury_(Hg)_pollution_in_sediment_cores_of_the_Thames_Estuary,_London,_UK.-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> \n</p><p>The highest amount of sedimentary-hosted Hg pollution in the Thames estuary occurs in the central London area between Vauxhall Bridge and Woolwich.<sup id="cite_ref-Rise_and_fall_of_Mercury_(Hg)_pollution_in_sediment_cores_of_the_Thames_Estuary,_London,_UK._69-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rise_and_fall_of_Mercury_(Hg)_pollution_in_sediment_cores_of_the_Thames_Estuary,_London,_UK.-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> The majority of sediment cores show a clear decrease in Hg concentrations close to the surface which is attributed to an overall reduction in polluting activities as well as improved effectiveness of recent environmental legalisation and river management (e.g. Oslo-Paris convention).\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Natural_carbon_compounds">Natural carbon compounds</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=River_Thames&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Natural carbon compounds">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Evaluation of select of <a href="/wiki/Lipid" title="Lipid">lipid</a> compounds in the Thames estuary, known as glycerol dialkyl glycerol tetraethers (<a href="/w/index.php?title=GDGTs&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="GDGTs (page does not exist)">GDGTs</a>) has revealed enhanced concentrations of isoprenoid GDGT compounds (crenarchaeol) around East London. This suggests that London\'s pollution affects the spatial distribution of natural carbon in the river sediments.<sup id="cite_ref-Signatures_of_tetraether_lipids_reveal_anthropogenic_overprinting_of_natural_organic_matter_in_sediments_of_the_Thames_estuary,_UK._71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Signatures_of_tetraether_lipids_reveal_anthropogenic_overprinting_of_natural_organic_matter_in_sediments_of_the_Thames_estuary,_UK.-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> Other organic geochemical measurements of carbon flow such as stable carbon isotopes (\xce\xb413C) were found to be insensitive to this urban disturbance.<sup id="cite_ref-Signatures_of_tetraether_lipids_reveal_anthropogenic_overprinting_of_natural_organic_matter_in_sediments_of_the_Thames_estuary,_UK._71-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Signatures_of_tetraether_lipids_reveal_anthropogenic_overprinting_of_natural_organic_matter_in_sediments_of_the_Thames_estuary,_UK.-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Sport">Sport</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=River_Thames&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Sport">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>There are several watersports prevalent on the Thames, with many clubs encouraging participation and organising racing and inter-club competitions.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rowing">Rowing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=River_Thames&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Rowing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Rowing_on_the_River_Thames" title="Rowing on the River Thames">Rowing on the River Thames</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Finish_of_2007_Oxford-Cambridge_boat_race.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Finish_of_2007_Oxford-Cambridge_boat_race.JPG/220px-Finish_of_2007_Oxford-Cambridge_boat_race.JPG" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Finish_of_2007_Oxford-Cambridge_boat_race.JPG/330px-Finish_of_2007_Oxford-Cambridge_boat_race.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Finish_of_2007_Oxford-Cambridge_boat_race.JPG/440px-Finish_of_2007_Oxford-Cambridge_boat_race.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3332" data-file-height="2221" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Finish_of_2007_Oxford-Cambridge_boat_race.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Cambridge cross the finish line ahead of Oxford in the 2007 <a href="/wiki/The_Boat_Race" title="The Boat Race">Boat Race</a>, viewed from Chiswick Bridge</div></div></div>\n<p>The Thames is the historic heartland of <a href="/wiki/Sport_rowing" class="mw-redirect" title="Sport rowing">rowing</a> in the United Kingdom. There are over 200 clubs on the river, and over 8,000 members of <a href="/wiki/British_Rowing" title="British Rowing">British Rowing</a> (over 40% of its membership).<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> Most towns and districts of any size on the river have at least one club. Internationally attended centres are <a href="/wiki/Oxford" title="Oxford">Oxford</a>, <a href="/wiki/Henley-on-Thames" title="Henley-on-Thames">Henley-on-Thames</a> and events and clubs on the stretch of river from <a href="/wiki/Chiswick" title="Chiswick">Chiswick</a> to <a href="/wiki/Putney" title="Putney">Putney</a>.\n</p><p>Two rowing events on the River Thames are traditionally part of the wider English sporting calendar:\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/The_Boat_Race" title="The Boat Race">University Boat Race</a> (between <a href="/wiki/Oxford_University_Boat_Club" title="Oxford University Boat Club">Oxford</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cambridge_University_Boat_Club" title="Cambridge University Boat Club">Cambridge</a>) takes place in late March or early April, on the <a href="/wiki/The_Championship_Course" title="The Championship Course">Championship Course</a> from <a href="/wiki/Putney" title="Putney">Putney</a> to <a href="/wiki/Mortlake" title="Mortlake">Mortlake</a> in the west of London.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Henley_Royal_Regatta" title="Henley Royal Regatta">Henley Royal Regatta</a> takes place over five days at the start of July in the upstream town of <a href="/wiki/Henley-on-Thames" title="Henley-on-Thames">Henley-on-Thames</a>. Besides its sporting significance the regatta is an important date on the English <a href="/wiki/Season_(society)" title="Season (society)">social calendar</a> alongside events like <a href="/wiki/Ascot_Racecourse" title="Ascot Racecourse">Royal Ascot</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wimbledon_Championships" class="mw-redirect" title="Wimbledon Championships">Wimbledon</a>.\n</p><p>Other significant or historic rowing events on the Thames include:\n</p>\n<ul><li>The <a href="/wiki/Head_of_the_River_Race" title="Head of the River Race">Head of the River Race</a> and <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_Eights_Head_of_the_River_Race" title="Women&#39;s Eights Head of the River Race">Women\'s Eights Head of the River Race</a> (8+) (i.e. coxed eights), <a href="/wiki/Schools%27_Head_of_the_River_Race" title="Schools&#39; Head of the River Race">Schools\' Head</a>, <a href="/wiki/Veterans_Head" class="mw-redirect" title="Veterans Head">Veterans Head</a>, <a href="/wiki/Scullers_Head_of_the_River_Race" title="Scullers Head of the River Race">Scullers Head</a>, <a href="/wiki/Head_of_the_River_Fours" title="Head of the River Fours">Fours Head (HOR4s)</a>, and Pairs Head (shorter) on the <a href="/wiki/The_Championship_Course" title="The Championship Course">Championship Course</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/The_Wingfield_Sculls" class="mw-redirect" title="The Wingfield Sculls">The Wingfield Sculls</a> on the same course: (1x) (<a href="/wiki/Single_scull" title="Single scull">single sculling</a>) championship</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Doggett%27s_Coat_and_Badge" title="Doggett&#39;s Coat and Badge">Doggett\'s Coat and Badge</a> for apprentice watermen of London, one of the oldest sporting events in the world</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Henley_Women%27s_Regatta" title="Henley Women&#39;s Regatta">Henley Women\'s Regatta</a></li>\n<li>The <a href="/wiki/Henley_Boat_Races" title="Henley Boat Races">Henley Boat Races</a> currently for the Lightweight (men\'s and women\'s) crews of Oxford and Cambridge universities</li>\n<li>The Oxford University <a href="/wiki/Bumps_race" title="Bumps race">bumping races</a> known as <a href="/wiki/Eights_Week" title="Eights Week">Eights Week</a> and <a href="/wiki/Torpids" title="Torpids">Torpids</a></li></ul>\n<p>Other <a href="/wiki/Regatta" title="Regatta">regattas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Head_race" title="Head race">head races</a> and university bumping races are held along the Thames which are described under <a href="/wiki/Rowing_on_the_River_Thames" title="Rowing on the River Thames">Rowing on the River Thames</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sailing">Sailing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=River_Thames&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Sailing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sailing_on_the_River_Thames" title="Sailing on the River Thames">Sailing on the River Thames</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:ThamesRaters01.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/ThamesRaters01.JPG/170px-ThamesRaters01.JPG" width="170" height="217" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/ThamesRaters01.JPG/255px-ThamesRaters01.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/ThamesRaters01.JPG/340px-ThamesRaters01.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1150" data-file-height="1470" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:ThamesRaters01.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Thames Raters at Raven\'s Ait, <a href="/wiki/Surbiton" title="Surbiton">Surbiton</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Sailing is practised on both the tidal and non-tidal reaches of the river. The highest club upstream is at Oxford. The most popular sailing craft used on the Thames are <a href="/wiki/Laser_(dinghy)" title="Laser (dinghy)">lasers</a>, <a href="/wiki/GP14_(dinghy)" class="mw-redirect" title="GP14 (dinghy)">GP14s</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wayfarer_(dinghy)" title="Wayfarer (dinghy)">Wayfarers</a>. One sailing boat unique to the Thames is the <a href="/wiki/Thames_A_Class_Rater_(scow)" class="mw-redirect" title="Thames A Class Rater (scow)">Thames Rater</a>, which is sailed around <a href="/wiki/Raven%27s_Ait" title="Raven&#39;s Ait">Raven\'s Ait</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Skiffing">Skiffing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=River_Thames&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Skiffing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Skiffing" title="Skiffing">Skiffing</a> has dwindled in favour of private motor boat ownership but is competed on the river in the summer months. Six clubs and a similar number of skiff regattas exist from <a href="/wiki/The_Skiff_Club" title="The Skiff Club">the Skiff Club</a>, <a href="/wiki/Teddington" title="Teddington">Teddington</a> upstream.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Punting">Punting</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=River_Thames&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Punting">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Unlike the "pleasure <a href="/wiki/Punt_(boat)" title="Punt (boat)">punting</a>" common on the <a href="/wiki/River_Cherwell" title="River Cherwell">Cherwell</a> in <a href="/wiki/Oxford" title="Oxford">Oxford</a> and the <a href="/wiki/River_Cam" title="River Cam">Cam</a> in <a href="/wiki/Cambridge" title="Cambridge">Cambridge</a>, punting on the Thames is competitive as well as recreational and uses narrower craft, typically based at the few skiff clubs.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Kayaking_and_canoeing">Kayaking and canoeing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=River_Thames&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Kayaking and canoeing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Kayaking_and_canoeing_on_the_River_Thames" title="Kayaking and canoeing on the River Thames">Kayaking and canoeing on the River Thames</a></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Kayaking" title="Kayaking">Kayaking</a> and <a href="/wiki/Canoeing" title="Canoeing">canoeing</a> are common, with <a href="/wiki/Sea_kayak" title="Sea kayak">sea kayakers</a> using the <a href="/wiki/Tidal_reach" class="mw-redirect" title="Tidal reach">tidal stretch</a> for touring. Sheltered water kayakers and canoeists use the non-tidal section for training, racing and trips. <a href="/wiki/Whitewater" title="Whitewater">Whitewater</a> <a href="/wiki/Playboating" title="Playboating">playboaters</a> and <a href="/wiki/Slalom_canoeing" class="mw-redirect" title="Slalom canoeing">slalom</a> paddlers are catered for at <a href="/wiki/Weir" title="Weir">weirs</a> like those at <a href="/wiki/Hurley_Lock" title="Hurley Lock">Hurley Lock</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sunbury_Lock" title="Sunbury Lock">Sunbury Lock</a> and <a href="/wiki/Boulter%27s_Lock" title="Boulter&#39;s Lock">Boulter\'s Lock</a>. At Teddington just before the tidal section of the river starts is <a href="/wiki/Royal_Canoe_Club" title="Royal Canoe Club">Royal Canoe Club</a>, said to be the oldest in the world and founded in 1866. Since 1950, almost every year at Easter, long distance canoeists have been competing in what is now known as the <a href="/wiki/Devizes_to_Westminster_International_Canoe_Race" class="mw-redirect" title="Devizes to Westminster International Canoe Race">Devizes to Westminster International Canoe Race</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> which follows the course of the <a href="/wiki/Kennet_and_Avon_Canal" title="Kennet and Avon Canal">Kennet and Avon Canal</a>, joins the River Thames at Reading and runs right up to a grand finish at <a href="/wiki/Westminster_Bridge" title="Westminster Bridge">Westminster Bridge</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Swimming">Swimming</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=River_Thames&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Swimming">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In 2006 British swimmer and environmental campaigner <a href="/wiki/Lewis_Pugh" title="Lewis Pugh">Lewis Pugh</a> became the first person to swim the full length of the Thames from outside Kemble to Southend-on-Sea to draw attention to the severe drought in England which saw record temperatures indicative of a degree of global warming. The 202 miles (325&#160;km) swim took him 21 days to complete. The official headwater of the river had stopped flowing due to the drought forcing Pugh to run the first 26 miles (42&#160;km).<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Since June 2012 the <a href="/wiki/Port_of_London_Authority" title="Port of London Authority">Port of London Authority</a> has made and enforces a <a href="/wiki/By-law" title="By-law">by-law</a> that bans swimming between Putney Bridge and <a href="/wiki/Crossness" title="Crossness">Crossness</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thamesmead" title="Thamesmead">Thamesmead</a> (thus including all of central London) without obtaining prior permission, on the grounds that swimmers in that area of the river endanger not only themselves, due to the strong current of the river, but also other river users.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Organised swimming events take place at various points generally upstream of <a href="/wiki/Hampton_Court" class="mw-redirect" title="Hampton Court">Hampton Court</a>, including Windsor, Marlow and Henley.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> In 2011 comedian <a href="/wiki/David_Walliams" title="David Walliams">David Walliams</a> swam the 140 miles (230&#160;km) from Lechlade to Westminster Bridge and raised over \xc2\xa31&#160;million for charity.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In non-tidal stretches swimming was,<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> and still is, a leisure and fitness activity among experienced swimmers where safe, deeper outer channels are used in times of low stream.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Meanders">Meanders</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=River_Thames&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Meanders">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>A <a href="/wiki/Thames_meander" title="Thames meander">Thames meander</a> is a long-distance journey over all or part of the Thames by running, swimming or using any of the above means. It is often carried out as an athletic challenge in a competition or for a record attempt.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Thames_in_the_arts">The Thames in the arts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=River_Thames&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: The Thames in the arts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Ambox_important.svg/40px-Ambox_important.svg.png" width="40" height="40" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Ambox_important.svg/60px-Ambox_important.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Ambox_important.svg/80px-Ambox_important.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="40" data-file-height="40" /></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This article <b>appears to contain <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:What_Wikipedia_is_not#Wikipedia_is_not_an_indiscriminate_collection_of_information" title="Wikipedia:What Wikipedia is not">trivial, minor, or unrelated</a> references to <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Trivia_sections#&quot;In_popular_culture&quot;_and_&quot;Cultural_references&quot;_material" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Trivia sections">popular culture</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please reorganize this content to explain the subject\'s impact on popular culture, using <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">references</a> to <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Identifying_reliable_sources" title="Wikipedia:Identifying reliable sources">reliable sources</a>, rather than simply listing appearances. class="gallerytext">\n<p><a href="/wiki/Claude_Monet" title="Claude Monet">Monet</a>\'s <i>Trou\xc3\xa9e de soleil dans le brouillard</i>, <a href="/wiki/Houses_of_Parliament_series_(Monet)" class="mw-redirect" title="Houses of Parliament series (Monet)">Houses of Parliament</a>, London, Sun Breaking Through the Fog, 1904\n</p>\n\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t</div></li>\n\t\t<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 235px"><div style="width: 235px">\n\t\t\t<div class="thumb" style="width: 230px;"><div style="margin:15px auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:James_Abbot_McNeill_Whistler_006.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/James_Abbot_McNeill_Whistler_006.jpg/116px-James_Abbot_McNeill_Whistler_006.jpg" width="116" height="154" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/James_Abbot_McNeill_Whistler_006.jpg/175px-James_Abbot_McNeill_Whistler_006.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/James_Abbot_McNeill_Whistler_006.jpg/233px-James_Abbot_McNeill_Whistler_006.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1124" data-file-height="1485" /></a></div></div>\n\t\t\t<div class="gallerytext">\n<p><a href="/wiki/James_Abbott_McNeill_Whistler" title="James Abbott McNeill Whistler">Whistler\'s</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Nocturne_in_Blue_and_Gold:_Old_Battersea_Bridge" class="mw-redirect" title="Nocturne in Blue and Gold: Old Battersea Bridge">Nocturne in Blue and Gold: Old Battersea Bridge</a></i> (c. 1872\xe2\x80\x931875)\n</p>\n\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t</div></li>\n\t\t<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 235px"><div style="width: 235px">\n\t\t\t<div class="thumb" style="width: 230px;"><div style="margin:35.5px auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:Brooklyn_Museum_-_Foggy_Morning_on_the_Thames_-_James_Hamilton_-_overall.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Brooklyn_Museum_-_Foggy_Morning_on_the_Thames_-_James_Hamilton_-_overall.jpg/200px-Brooklyn_Museum_-_Foggy_Morning_on_the_Thames_-_James_Hamilton_-_overall.jpg" width="200" height="113" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Brooklyn_Museum_-_Foggy_Morning_on_the_Thames_-_James_Hamilton_-_overall.jpg/300px-Brooklyn_Museum_-_Foggy_Morning_on_the_Thames_-_James_Hamilton_-_overall.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Brooklyn_Museum_-_Foggy_Morning_on_the_Thames_-_James_Hamilton_-_overall.jpg/400px-Brooklyn_Museum_-_Foggy_Morning_on_the_Thames_-_James_Hamilton_-_overall.jpg 2x" data-file-width="768" data-file-height="432" /></a></div></div>\n\t\t\t<div class="gallerytext">\n<p><i>Foggy Morning on the Thames</i> \xe2\x80\x93 James Hamilton (between 1872 and 1878)\n</p>\n\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t</div></li>\n\t\t<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 235px"><div style="width: 235px">\n\t\t\t<div class="thumb" style="width: 230px;"><div style="margin:15px auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:Boating_on_the_Thames_by_John_Lavery.jpeg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Boating_on_the_Thames_by_John_Lavery.jpeg/183px-Boating_on_the_Thames_by_John_Lavery.jpeg" width="183" height="154" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Boating_on_the_Thames_by_John_Lavery.jpeg/275px-Boating_on_the_Thames_by_John_Lavery.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Boating_on_the_Thames_by_John_Lavery.jpeg/366px-Boating_on_the_Thames_by_John_Lavery.jpeg 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="841" /></a></div></div>\n\t\t\t<div class="gallerytext">\n<p><i>Boating on the Thames</i> - <a href="/wiki/John_Lavery" title="John Lavery">John Lavery</a>, circa 1890\n</p>\n\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t</div></li>\n</ul>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Visual_arts">Visual arts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=River_Thames&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Visual arts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The River Thames has been a subject for artists, great and minor, over the centuries. Four major artists with works based on the Thames are <a href="/wiki/Canaletto" title="Canaletto">Canaletto</a>, <a href="/wiki/J._M._W._Turner" title="J. M. W. Turner">J. M. W. Turner</a>, <a href="/wiki/Claude_Monet" title="Claude Monet">Claude Monet</a> and <a href="/wiki/James_Abbott_McNeill_Whistler" title="James Abbott McNeill Whistler">James Abbott McNeill Whistler</a>. The 20th-century British artist <a href="/wiki/Stanley_Spencer" title="Stanley Spencer">Stanley Spencer</a> produced many works at <a href="/wiki/Cookham" title="Cookham">Cookham</a>.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/John_Kaufman" class="mw-redirect" title="John Kaufman">John Kaufman</a>\'s sculpture <i><a href="/wiki/The_Diver" title="The Diver">The Diver: Regeneration</a></i> is sited in the Thames near <a href="/wiki/Rainham,_London" title="Rainham, London">Rainham</a>.\n</p><p>The river and bridges are portrayed as being destroyed \xe2\x80\x93 together with much of London \xe2\x80\x93 in the film <i><a href="/wiki/Independence_Day_2" class="mw-redirect" title="Independence Day 2">Independence Day 2</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Literature">Literature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=River_Thames&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: Literature">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Seal_St._Saviour%27s_Dock_London.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Seal_St._Saviour%27s_Dock_London.jpg/220px-Seal_St._Saviour%27s_Dock_London.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Seal_St._Saviour%27s_Dock_London.jpg/330px-Seal_St._Saviour%27s_Dock_London.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Seal_St._Saviour%27s_Dock_London.jpg/440px-Seal_St._Saviour%27s_Dock_London.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5616" data-file-height="3744" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Seal_St._Saviour%27s_Dock_London.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A seal in the river at St. Saviour\'s Dock, London</div></div></div>\n<p>The Thames is mentioned in many works of literature including novels, diaries and poetry. It is the central theme in three in particular:\n</p><p><i><a href="/wiki/Three_Men_in_a_Boat" title="Three Men in a Boat">Three Men in a Boat</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Jerome_K._Jerome" title="Jerome K. Jerome">Jerome K. Jerome</a>, first published in 1889, is a humorous account of a boating holiday on the Thames between <a href="/wiki/Kingston_upon_Thames" title="Kingston upon Thames">Kingston</a> and <a href="/wiki/Oxford" title="Oxford">Oxford</a>. The book was intended initially to be a serious travel guide, with accounts of local history of places along the route, but the humorous elements eventually took over. The landscape and features of the Thames as described by Jerome are virtually unchanged, and the book\'s enduring popularity has meant that it has never been out of print since it was first published.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Charles_Dickens" title="Charles Dickens">Charles Dickens</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Our_Mutual_Friend" title="Our Mutual Friend">Our Mutual Friend</a></i> (written in the years 1864\xe2\x80\x9365) describes the river in a grimmer light. It begins with a scavenger and his daughter pulling a dead man from the river near London Bridge, to salvage what the body might have in its pockets, and leads to its conclusion with the deaths of the villains drowned in <a href="/wiki/Shepperton_Lock" title="Shepperton Lock">Plashwater Lock</a> upstream. The workings of the river and the influence of the tides are described with great accuracy. Dickens opens the novel with this sketch of the river, and the people who work on it:\n</p>\n<blockquote>\n<p><i>In these times of ours, though concerning the exact year there is no need to be precise, a boat of dirty and disreputable appearance, with two figures in it, floated on the Thames, between <a href="/wiki/Southwark_Bridge" title="Southwark Bridge">Southwark Bridge</a> which is of iron, and <a href="/wiki/London_Bridge" title="London Bridge">London Bridge</a> which is of stone, as an autumn evening was closing in.</i>\n<i>The figures in this boat were those of a strong man with ragged grizzled hair and a sun-browned face, and a girl of nineteen or twenty. The girl rowed, pulling a pair of sculls very easily; the man with the rudder-lines slack in his hands, and his hands loose in his waisteband, kept an eager look-out.</i>\n</p>\n</blockquote>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Kenneth_Grahame" title="Kenneth Grahame">Kenneth Grahame</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Wind_in_the_Willows" title="The Wind in the Willows">The Wind in the Willows</a></i>, written in 1908, is set in the middle to upper reaches of the river. It starts as a tale of anthropomorphic characters "simply messing about in boats" but develops into a more complex story combining elements of mysticism with adventure and reflection on <a href="/wiki/Edwardian_era" title="Edwardian era">Edwardian</a> society. It is generally considered one of the most beloved works of children\'s literature<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> and the illustrations by E.H.Shepard and Arthur Rackham feature the Thames and its surroundings.\n</p><p>The river almost inevitably features in many books set in London. Most of Dickens\' other novels include some aspect of the Thames. <i><a href="/wiki/Oliver_Twist" title="Oliver Twist">Oliver Twist</a></i> finishes in the slums and <a href="/wiki/Rookery_(slum)" title="Rookery (slum)">rookeries</a> along its south bank. The <a href="/wiki/Sherlock_Holmes" title="Sherlock Holmes">Sherlock Holmes</a> stories by <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Conan_Doyle" title="Arthur Conan Doyle">Arthur Conan Doyle</a> often visit riverside parts as in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Sign_of_the_Four" title="The Sign of the Four">The Sign of Four</a></i>. In <i><a href="/wiki/Heart_of_Darkness" title="Heart of Darkness">Heart of Darkness</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Conrad" title="Joseph Conrad">Joseph Conrad</a>, the serenity of the contemporary Thames is contrasted with the savagery of the <a href="/wiki/Congo_River" title="Congo River">Congo River</a>, and with the wilderness of the Thames as it would have appeared to a Roman soldier posted to Britannia two thousand years before. Conrad also gives a description of the approach to London from the <a href="/wiki/Thames_Estuary" title="Thames Estuary">Thames Estuary</a> in his essays <i><a href="https://en.wikiquote.org/wiki/Joseph_Conrad#On_the_River_Thames" class="extiw" title="q:Joseph Conrad">The Mirror of the Sea</a></i> (1906). Upriver, <a href="/wiki/Henry_James" title="Henry James">Henry James</a>\' <i><a href="/wiki/Portrait_of_a_Lady" class="mw-redirect" title="Portrait of a Lady">Portrait of a Lady</a></i> uses a large riverside mansion on the Thames as one of its key settings.\n</p><p>Literary non-fiction works include <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Pepys" title="Samuel Pepys">Samuel Pepys</a>\' diary, in which he recorded many events relating to the Thames including the <a href="/wiki/Fire_of_London" class="mw-redirect" title="Fire of London">Fire of London</a>. He was disturbed while writing it in June 1667 by the sound of gunfire as Dutch warships broke through the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Navy" title="Royal Navy">Royal Navy</a> on the Thames.\n</p><p>In poetry, <a href="/wiki/William_Wordsworth" title="William Wordsworth">William Wordsworth</a>\'s sonnet <a href="/wiki/Composed_upon_Westminster_Bridge,_September_3,_1802" title="Composed upon Westminster Bridge, September 3, 1802">On Westminster Bridge</a> closes with the lines:\n</p>\n<dl><dd>Ne\'er saw I, never felt, a calm so deep!</dd>\n<dd>The river glideth at his own sweet will:</dd>\n<dd>Dear God! the very houses seem asleep;</dd>\n<dd>And all that mighty heart is lying still!</dd></dl>\n<p><a href="/wiki/T._S._Eliot" title="T. S. Eliot">T. S. Eliot</a> makes several references to the Thames in <i>The Fire Sermon, Section III</i> of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Waste_Land" title="The Waste Land">The Waste Land</a></i>.\n</p>\n<dl><dd>Sweet Thames run softly, till I end my song.</dd>\n<dd>The river bears no empty bottles, sandwich papers,</dd>\n<dd>Silk handkerchiefs, cardboard boxes cigarette ends</dd>\n<dd>Or other testimony of summer nights.</dd></dl>\n<p>and\n</p>\n<dl><dd>The river sweats</dd>\n<dd>Oil and tar</dd>\n<dd>The barges drift</dd>\n<dd>With the turning tide</dd>\n<dd>Red sails</dd>\n<dd>Wide</dd>\n<dd>To leeward, swing on the heavy spar,</dd>\n<dd>The barges wash</dd>\n<dd>Drifting logs</dd>\n<dd>Down Greenwich reach</dd>\n<dd>Past the Isle of Dogs</dd></dl>\n<p>The <i>Sweet Thames</i> line is taken from <a href="/wiki/Edmund_Spenser" title="Edmund Spenser">Edmund Spenser</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Prothalamion" title="Prothalamion">Prothalamion</a></i> which presents a more idyllic image:\n</p>\n<dl><dd>Along the shoare of silver streaming Themmes;</dd>\n<dd>Whose rutty banke, the which his river hemmes,</dd>\n<dd>Was paynted all with variable flowers.</dd>\n<dd>And all the meads adornd with daintie gemmes</dd>\n<dd>Fit to deck maydens bowres</dd></dl>\n<p>Also writing of the upper reaches is <a href="/wiki/Matthew_Arnold" title="Matthew Arnold">Matthew Arnold</a> in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Scholar_Gypsy" class="mw-redirect" title="The Scholar Gypsy">The Scholar Gypsy</a></i>:\n</p>\n<dl><dd>Crossing the stripling Thames at Bab-lock-hythe</dd>\n<dd>Trailing in the cool stream thy fingers wet</dd>\n<dd>As the slow punt swings round</dd></dl>\n<dl><dd>Oh born in days when wits were fresh and clear</dd>\n<dd>And life ran gaily as the sparkling Thames;</dd>\n<dd>Before this strange disease of modern life.</dd></dl>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Wendy_Cope" title="Wendy Cope">Wendy Cope</a>\'s poem \'After the Lunch\' is set on Waterloo Bridge, beginning:\n</p>\n<dl><dd>On Waterloo Bridge, where we said our goodbyes,</dd>\n<dd>The weather conditions bring tears to my eyes.</dd>\n<dd>I wipe them away with a black woolly glove,</dd>\n<dd>And try not to notice I\xe2\x80\x99ve fallen in love.</dd></dl>\n<p>Dylan Thomas mentions the Thames in his poem "A Refusal to Mourn the Death, by Fire, of a Child in London". "London\'s Daughter", the subject of the poem, lays "Deep with the first dead...secret by the unmourning water of the riding Thames".\n</p><p>Science-fiction novels make liberal use of a futuristic Thames. The utopian <i><a href="/wiki/News_from_Nowhere" title="News from Nowhere">News from Nowhere</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/William_Morris" title="William Morris">William Morris</a> is mainly the account of a journey through the <a href="/wiki/Thames_valley" class="mw-redirect" title="Thames valley">Thames valley</a> in a socialist future. The Thames also features prominently in <a href="/wiki/Philip_Pullman" title="Philip Pullman">Philip Pullman</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/His_Dark_Materials" title="His Dark Materials">His Dark Materials</a></i> trilogy, as a communications artery for the waterborne Gyptian people of Oxford and the <a href="/wiki/Fens" class="mw-redirect" title="Fens">Fens</a>, and as a prominent setting for his novel <a href="/wiki/La_Belle_Sauvage" title="La Belle Sauvage">La Belle Sauvage</a>.\n</p><p>In <i><a href="/wiki/The_Deptford_Mice" title="The Deptford Mice">The Deptford Mice</a></i> trilogy by <a href="/wiki/Robin_Jarvis" title="Robin Jarvis">Robin Jarvis</a>, the Thames appears several times. In one book, rat characters swim through it to <a href="/wiki/Deptford" title="Deptford">Deptford</a>. Winner of the <a href="/wiki/Nestl%C3%A9_Smarties_Book_Prize" title="Nestl\xc3\xa9 Smarties Book Prize">Nestl\xc3\xa9 Children\'s Book Prize</a> Gold Award <i><a href="/wiki/I,_Coriander" title="I, Coriander">I, Coriander</a></i>, by Sally Gardner is a fantasy novel in which the heroine lives on the banks of the Thames.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Mark_Wallington_(writer)" title="Mark Wallington (writer)">Mark Wallington</a> describes a journey up the Thames in a camping skiff, in his 1989 book <i>Boogie up the River</i> (<a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-09-965910-5" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-09-965910-5">978-0-09-965910-5</a>).\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Music">Music</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=River_Thames&amp;action=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: Music">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Water_Music_(Handel)" class="mw-redirect" title="Water Music (Handel)">Water Music</a> composed by <a href="/wiki/George_Frideric_Handel" title="George Frideric Handel">George Frideric Handel</a> premiered on 17 July 1717, when <a href="/wiki/George_I_of_Great_Britain" title="George I of Great Britain">King George I</a> requested a concert on the River Thames. The concert was performed for King George I on his barge and he is said to have enjoyed it so much that he ordered the 50 exhausted musicians to play the suites three times on the trip.\n</p><p>The song \'Old Father Thames\' was recorded by <a href="/wiki/Peter_Dawson_(bass-baritone)" title="Peter Dawson (bass-baritone)">Peter Dawson</a> at <a href="/wiki/Abbey_Road_Studios" title="Abbey Road Studios">Abbey Road Studios</a> in 1933 and by <a href="/wiki/Gracie_Fields" title="Gracie Fields">Gracie Fields</a> five years later.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Jessie_Matthews" title="Jessie Matthews">Jessie Matthews</a> sings "My river" in the 1938 film <i><a href="/wiki/Sailing_Along" title="Sailing Along">Sailing Along</a></i>, and the tune is the centrepiece of a major dance number near the end of the film.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Sex_Pistols" title="Sex Pistols">Sex Pistols</a> played a concert on the <i>Queen Elizabeth Riverboat</i> on 7 June 1977, the Queen\'s Silver Jubilee year, while sailing down the river.\n</p><p>The choral line "(I) <i>(liaised)</i> live by the river" in the song "<a href="/wiki/London_Calling" title="London Calling">London Calling</a>" by <a href="/wiki/The_Clash" title="The Clash">the Clash</a> refers to the River Thames.\n</p><p>Two songs by <a href="/wiki/The_Kinks" title="The Kinks">the Kinks</a> feature the Thames as the setting of the first song\'s title and, for the second song, arguably in its mention of \'the river\': "<a href="/wiki/Waterloo_Sunset" title="Waterloo Sunset">Waterloo Sunset</a>" is about a couple\'s meetings on <a href="/wiki/Waterloo_Bridge" title="Waterloo Bridge">Waterloo Bridge</a>, London and starts: "Dirty old river, must you keep rolling, flowing into the night?" and continues "Terry meets Julie, <a href="/wiki/London_Waterloo_station" title="London Waterloo station">Waterloo station</a>" and "...but Terry and Julie cross over the river where they feel safe and sound...". "<a href="/wiki/See_My_Friends" title="See My Friends">See My Friends</a>" continually refers to the singer\'s friends "playing \'cross the river" instead of the girl who "just left". Furthermore, <a href="/wiki/Ray_Davies" title="Ray Davies">Ray Davies</a> as a solo artist refers to the river Thames in his "London Song".<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Ewan_MacColl" title="Ewan MacColl">Ewan MacColl</a>\'s "Sweet Thames, Flow Softly", written in the early 1960s, is a tragic love ballad set on trip up the river (see <a href="/wiki/Edmund_Spencer" class="mw-redirect" title="Edmund Spencer">Edmund Spencer</a>\'s love poem\'s refrain above)\n</p><p>English musician <a href="/wiki/Imogen_Heap" title="Imogen Heap">Imogen Heap</a> wrote a song from the point of view of the River Thames entitled "You Know Where To Find Me". The song was released in 2012 on 18 October as the sixth single from her fourth album <a href="/wiki/Sparks_(Imogen_Heap_album)" title="Sparks (Imogen Heap album)">Sparks</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Major_flood_events">Major flood events</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=River_Thames&amp;action=edit&amp;section=46" title="Edit section: Major flood events">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="London_flood_of_1928">London flood of 1928</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=River_Thames&amp;action=edit&amp;section=47" title="Edit section: London flood of 1928">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/1928_Thames_flood" title="1928 Thames flood">1928 Thames flood</a></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/1928_Thames_flood" title="1928 Thames flood">1928 Thames flood</a> was a disastrous flood of the River Thames that affected much of riverside London on 7 January 1928, as well as places further downriver. Fourteen people were drowned in London and thousands were made homeless when flood waters poured over the top of the <a href="/wiki/Thames_Embankment" title="Thames Embankment">Thames Embankment</a> and part of the <a href="/wiki/Chelsea_Embankment" title="Chelsea Embankment">Chelsea Embankment</a> collapsed. It was the last major flood to affect <a href="/wiki/Central_London" title="Central London">central London</a>, and, particularly following the disastrous <a href="/wiki/North_Sea_flood_of_1953" title="North Sea flood of 1953">North Sea flood of 1953</a>, helped lead to the implementation of new flood-control measures that culminated in the construction of the <a href="/wiki/Thames_Barrier" title="Thames Barrier">Thames Barrier</a> in the 1970s.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Thames_Valley_flood_of_1947">Thames Valley flood of 1947</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=River_Thames&amp;action=edit&amp;section=48" title="Edit section: Thames Valley flood of 1947">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/1947_Thames_flood" title="1947 Thames flood">1947 Thames flood</a></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/1947_Thames_flood" title="1947 Thames flood">1947 Thames flood</a> was worst overall 20th-century flood of the River Thames, affecting much of the <a href="/wiki/Thames_Valley" title="Thames Valley">Thames Valley</a> as well as elsewhere in England during the middle of March 1947 after a very <a href="/wiki/Winter_of_1946%E2%80%931947" class="mw-redirect" title="Winter of 1946\xe2\x80\x931947">severe winter</a>.\n</p><p>The floods were caused by 4.6 inches (120&#160;mm) of rainfall (including snow); the peak flow was 61.7&#160;billion litres (13.6&#160;billion imperial gallons) of water per day and the damage cost a total of \xc2\xa312&#160;million to repair.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup>\n<a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">War</a> damage to some of the <a href="/wiki/Lock_(water_transport)" class="mw-redirect" title="Lock (water transport)">locks</a> made matters worse.\n</p><p>Other significant Thames floods since 1947 have occurred in 1968, 1993, 1998, 2000, 2003, 2006 and 2014.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Canvey_Island_flood_of_1953">Canvey Island flood of 1953</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=River_Thames&amp;action=edit&amp;section=49" title="Edit section: Canvey Island flood of 1953">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Canvey_Island" title="Canvey Island">Canvey Island</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Water_canvey.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Water_canvey.jpg/220px-Water_canvey.jpg" width="220" height="158" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Water_canvey.jpg/330px-Water_canvey.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Water_canvey.jpg/440px-Water_canvey.jpg 2x" data-file-width="768" data-file-height="550" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Water_canvey.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The flooded <a href="/wiki/Canvey_Island" title="Canvey Island">Canvey Island</a> sea front, amusements and residential areas in 1953</div></div></div>\n<p>On the night of 31 January, the <a href="/wiki/North_Sea_flood_of_1953" title="North Sea flood of 1953">North Sea flood of 1953</a> devastated the island taking the lives of 58 islanders, and led to the temporary evacuation of the 13,000 residents.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> Canvey is consequently protected by modern sea defences comprising 15 miles (24&#160;km) of concrete seawall.<sup id="cite_ref-drainage_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-drainage-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> Many of the victims were in the holiday bungalows of the eastern Newlands estate and perished as the water reached ceiling level. The small village area of the island is approximately two feet (0.6&#160;m) above sea level and consequently escaped the effects of the flood.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See
